import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 2D convolution with a square input and an asymmetric kernel.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple): Size of the convolution kernel (height, width).
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int or tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        output_padding (int or tuple, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to 0.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose2d = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = (3, 7)  # larger asymmetric kernel
width = 512
height = 512

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

The transpose (adjoint) maps output_grad to input_grad: input_grad[i,j] = sum_{p,q} output_grad[i-p, j-q] * weight[p,q]. That is a full convolution (with padding kernel_size-1) of output_grad with weight (non-flipped?). The transpose of cross-correlation is cross-correlation with the same weight but applied with full padding. Wait, conv1d on input [a, b] with kernel [w0, w1]: output[0] = a*w0 + b*w1 Hmm, these don't match. Let me reconsider. Actually, for conv_transpose1d: The relationship is: if conv(input, weight, stride) = output, then input ≈ conv_transpose(output, weight, stride). For stride=1, conv_transpose is the adjoint of conv. In 1D: - conv: y[n] = sum_k x[n+k] * w[k] (with appropriate indexing) - conv_transpose: z[n] = sum_k y[n-k] * w[k] Wait, let me think about this differently. PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, i, j} input[n, c_in, h - i, w - j] * weight[c_in, c_out, i, j] where negative indices are clamped/ignored. For input of shape (H, W) and kernel (K_h, K_w), output is (H+K_h-1, W+K_w-1). This is a full convolution (cross-correlation without flipping actually, since PyTorch uses cross-correlation in Conv2d, and ConvTranspose2d is its transpose). Actually, for Conv2d pytorch does cross-correlation: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, i, j} input[n, c_in, h+i, w+j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] For ConvTranspose2d as gradient w.r.t. input of Conv2d: grad_input[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, i, j} grad_output[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] With h-i replaced appropriately. For stride=1, padding=0 in the forward conv: grad_input[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, i, j} grad_output[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] = sum_{c_out, i, j} grad_output[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] If grad_output is our input to ConvTranspose2d: output[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, i, j} input[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] = sum_{c_out, i, j} input[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] Now transpose indices to get standard form: Let h' = h - i, w' = w - j, so i = h - h', j = w - w' output[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, h', w'} input[n, c_out, h', w'] * weight[c_out, c_in, h-h', w-w'] So for ConvTranspose2d, the "forward" operation treats the weight as if indexed by weight[c_out, c_in, h-h', w-w']. Since PyTorch ConvTranspose2d stores weight as (in_channels, out_channels/groups, K_h, K_w), which is (64, 64, 3, 7), this is equivalent to computing a convolution where the "output" channel dimension is indexed by c_in and the effective weight would be weight[c_out, c_in, ...] if we think of it as conv. Actually, for our direct kernel implementation, the simplest approach is: - The output has shape (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) where H_out = H_in + K_h - 1, W_out = W_in + K_w - 1 - Each output pixel at (h, w) receives from input positions: h_in = h - dh, w_in = w - dw where dh ∈ [0, K_h), dw ∈ [0, K_w) and we require 0 ≤ h_in < H_in, 0 ≤ w_in < W_in - The accumulation is: output[n, c_out, h, w] += input[n, c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] So this is a lot like a regular convolution but with different weight indexing (c_in, c_out instead of c_out, c_in) and without flipping the kernel spatially. For performance, I can write this as a tiled kernel. Let me design it. Given the sizes, I should think about: 1. How to tile in C_in and C_out dimensions for parallelism 2. How to tile in spatial dimensions 3. How to use shared memory for input and weight Actually, a simpler and often faster approach for moderate channel counts is to use a "direct" convolution where each thread computes one output pixel across all channels, or a small tile. With 514 * 518 ≈ 266K output spatial positions per image, and 8 images = 2.1M total spatial positions. With 64 output channels, that's 136M output elements. If each block computes a spatial tile of e.g., 8x8 or 16x16 output pixels, and all output channels, we have good parallelism. Actually, a common optimization is to compute multiple output channels per thread. Let's say: - Block size: 16x16 threads - Each thread computes 1 output pixel and processes all C_out channels in a loop over C_in tiles Wait, no. Each output pixel for each c_out is an independent accumulation over c_in. Maybe structure it as: - Grid: (N, C_out_tiles, H_out_tiles, W_out_tiles) - Each block computes a tile of output for specific (n, c_out_range, h_range, w_range) For example, a block computes 8x8 spatial output pixels for 8 output channels. Let's define: - TileH = 8, TileW = 8, TileC_out = 8 - Block = (TileH, TileW, TileC_out) = (8, 8, 8) = 512 threads Given 64 output channels, we'd have 8 channel tiles. Given 514x518 output spatial, we'd have ceil(514/8) * ceil(518/8) = 65 * 65 = 4225 spatial tiles per image and channel tile. Total blocks: 8 (N) * 8 (C_out tiles) * 4225 = 270,400 blocks. That's plenty. Now for shared memory: - Each block loads input for its spatial tile plus halo: (8+3-1) x (8+7-1) = 10 x 14 spatial region, for a tile of C_in channels. - Each block loads weight for the C_out tile and C_in tile: 8 x 8 x 3 x 7 = 1344 floats. If we process C_in in chunks of, say, TileC_in = 8: - Load input: 10 * 14 * 8 = 1120 floats - Load weight: 8 * 8 * 3 * 7 = 1344 floats - Total shared memory: (1120 + 1344) * 4 bytes = ~9.8KB per block. Very manageable. Actually, but the weight doesn't need to be loaded in a C_in tile, we can just load what we need. Wait, weight is [C_in, C_out, K_h, K_w]. For a block computing C_out tile [c_start:c_start+8], and for each c_in in [0,64), it needs weight[c_in, c_start:c_start+8, :, :]. If we process c_in in tiles of 8, we load weight[8, 8, 3, 7] = 1344 floats. Good. For input, computing output tile [h:h+8, w:w+8] requires input [h:h+8+2, w:w+8+6] = [h:h+10, w:w+14]. For C_in tile of 8, that's 10*14*8 = 1120 floats. Shared memory usage: (1120 + 1344) * 4 = ~9.8KB. This is great. Let me outline the kernel: ```cuda template<int TileH, int TileW, int TileC_in, int TileC_out> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int K_h, int K_w ) { // Block indices int n = blockIdx.x; int c_out_block = blockIdx.y; int h_block = blockIdx.z / gridDim.z_w; // Need to set up grid properly int w_block = blockIdx.z % gridDim.z_w; // Thread indices int th = threadIdx.x; // 0..TileH-1 int tw = threadIdx.y; // 0..TileW-1 int tc = threadIdx.z; // 0..TileC_out-1 // Output coordinates int h_out = h_block * TileH + th; int w_out = w_block * TileW + tw; int c_out = c_out_block * TileC_out + tc; if (c_out >= C_out) return; // Accumulator float acc = 0.0f; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; float* smem_weight = smem + (TileH + K_h - 1) * (TileW + K_w - 1) * TileC_in; // Loop over C_in tiles for (int c_in_block = 0; c_in_block < (C_in + TileC_in - 1) / TileC_in; ++c_in_block) { // Load input tile into shared memory int c_in_start = c_in_block * TileC_in; int H_load = TileH + K_h - 1; int W_load = TileW + K_w - 1; int h_in_start = h_block * TileH; int w_in_start = w_block * TileW; // Each thread loads multiple elements int input_smem_size = H_load * W_load * TileC_in; for (int idx = th * TileW * TileC_in + tw * TileC_in + tc; idx < input_smem_size; idx += TileH * TileW * TileC_out) { // map idx to (h, w, c) in smem int c = idx % TileC_in; int tmp = idx / TileC_in; int w = tmp % W_load; int h = tmp / W_load; int c_in = c_in_start + c; int h_in = h_in_start + h; int w_in = w_in_start + w; float val = 0.0f; if (c_in < C_in && h_in < H_in && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; } smem_input[((h * W_load) + w) * TileC_in + c] = val; } // Load weight tile into shared memory int weight_smem_size = K_h * K_w * TileC_out * TileC_in; for (int idx = th * TileW * TileC_in + tw * TileC_out + tc; idx < weight_smem_size; idx += TileH * TileW * TileC_out) { int c_in = idx % TileC_in; float val = 0.0f; if (c_in_global < C_in && c_out_global < C_out) { val = weight[((c_in_global * C_out + c_out_global) * K_h + h) * K_w + w]; } smem_weight[(((h * K_w + w) * TileC_out) + c_out_local) * TileC_in + c_in] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial sum // For output (h_out, w_out, c_out), sum over c_in, dh, dw // input at (h_out - dh, w_out - dw) * weight[dh, dw] where h_out - dh is in input // But wait, we loaded input for [h_in_start : h_in_start + H_load] // and output h_out = h_block * TileH + th // input needed: h_out - dh for dh in [0, K_h) // = h_block * TileH + th - dh // h_in_start = h_block * TileH // So input index in smem: (th - dh + K_h - 1)? No wait. // // For conv_transpose2d: output[h_out, w_out] += input[h_out - dh, w_out - dw] * weight[dh, dw] // We loaded input starting at h_in_start = h_block * TileH // The input position h_out - dh = h_block * TileH + th - dh // Since dh in [0, K_h), th - dh ranges from th - (K_h - 1) to th // Wait, th ranges from 0 to TileH-1, so th - dh ranges from -(K_h-1) to TileH-1 // But we loaded H_load = TileH + K_h - 1 elements starting at h_in_start // Specifically, [h_in_start, h_in_start + H_load - 1] = [h_block*TileH, h_block*TileH + TileH + K_h - 2] // The input position h_block*TileH + th - dh is in range: // min: h_block*TileH - (K_h-1) -- BUT we only loaded starting at h_block*TileH ! // // Hmm, this is wrong. Let me reconsider. // // output[h_out] += input[h_out - dh] * weight[dh] // For h_out = h_block * TileH + th: // input needed: h_block * TileH + th - dh // // We loaded input starting at h_in_start. But what is h_in_start? // If we set h_in_start = h_block * TileH - (K_h - 1), then we'd cover: // [h_block*TileH - K_h + 1, h_block*TileH - K_h + 1 + TileH + K_h - 2] = [h_block*TileH - K_h + 1, h_block*TileH + TileH - 1] // This includes h_block*TileH + th - dh for dh in [0, K_h-1] and th in [0, TileH-1]. // // Actually for conv2d (not transpose), if output[h] += sum_dh input[h + dh] * weight[dh] // then for tile starting at h_block*TileH, we need input[h_block*TileH + th + dh] for dh in [0, K_h-1] // = [h_block*TileH, h_block*TileH + TileH - 1 + K_h - 1] // So load starting at h_block*TileH, size TileH + K_h - 1. // // For conv_transpose2d: output[h] += sum_dh input[h - dh] * weight[dh] // For tile starting at h_block*TileH, we need input[h_block*TileH + th - dh] for dh in [0, K_h-1] // = [h_block*TileH - (K_h-1), h_block*TileH + TileH - 1] // So load starting at h_block*TileH - (K_h-1), size TileH + K_h - 1. // // Ah, this is different. For ConvTranspose2d in PyTorch: - weight shape: (in_channels, out_channels/groups, *kernel_size) The formula is: output[N, C_out, H_out, W_out] = sum_{k=0}^{C_in-1} sum_{s=0}^{K_h-1} sum_{t=0}^{K_w-1} input[N, k, H_out - s - 1 + pad_h, W_out - t - 1 + pad_w] * weight[k, C_out, s, t] Wait, no, that's for general stride. For stride=1: output[N, C_out, H_out, W_out] = sum_{k=0}^{C_in-1} sum_{s=0}^{K_h-1} sum_{t=0}^{K_w-1} input[N, k, stride*H_out + s - pad_h, ...] ... no. Let me look up the actual PyTorch formula. For ConvTranspose2d: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K_h - 1) + output_padding + 1 For stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, output_padding=0: H_out = H_in - 1 + K_h - 1 + 1 = H_in + K_h - 1 The operation is: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, (h + pad_h - dh) / stride, (w + pad_w - dw) / stride] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where the division is integer and only included when (h + pad_h - dh) % stride == 0 and indices are valid. For stride=1, padding=0: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh=0}^{K_h-1} sum_{dw=0}^{K_w-1} input[n, c_in, h - dh, w - dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where input indices must be >= 0 and < H_in, W_in. This is a "full" cross-correlation (without flipping the kernel). For a regular conv2d, the formula is: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, h + dh, w + dw] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] So conv_transpose2d with stride=1, padding=0 is: 1. No flipping of kernel spatially (same as cross-correlation) Actually, if we define flipped_weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] = weight[c_in, c_out, K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw], then: output[h, w] = sum input[h-dh, w-dw] * weight[dh, dw] = sum input[h', w'] * weight[h-h', w-w'] (with h' = h-dh) = sum input[h', w'] * flipped_weight[K_h-1-(h-h'), K_w-1-(w-w')] Wait, this is getting messy. Let me just implement the direct formula for conv_transpose2d. Actually, wait. Let me double check with actual PyTorch which indexing convention they use for conv_transpose2d. In PyTorch, conv2d does cross-correlation: y[h,w] = sum_{i,j} x[h+i, w+j] * w[i,j] For conv_transpose2d, it's the transpose operation. In matrix form, if y = A * x (where A represents convolution), then conv_transpose2d computes A^T * y. For a 1D example with kernel [w0, w1], stride=1, no padding: Conv: y[0] = x[0]*w0 + x[1]*w1, y[1] = x[1]*w0 + x[2]*w1 (for input [x0, x1, x2]) Actually for input size 3, output size 2 with kernel 2: y[0] = x[0]*w0 + x[1]*w1 y[1] = x[1]*w0 + x[2]*w1 Matrix A (2x3): [w0, w1, 0] [ 0, w0, w1] A^T (3x2): [w0, 0] [w1, w0] [ 0, w1] So conv_transpose2d on input [y0, y1] gives: out[0] = y0*w0 out[1] = y0*w1 + y1*w0 out[2] = y1*w1 This is: out[h] = sum_{dh} y[h - dh] * w[dh] where valid dh are those where 0 <= h - dh < input_size and 0 <= dh < kernel_size. For h=0: dh=0, out[0] = y[0]*w[0] For h=1: dh=0 gives y[1]*w[0], dh=1 gives y[0]*w[1] For h=2: dh=1 gives y[1]*w[1] So yes, the formula is: output[h] = sum_{dh} input[h - dh] * weight[dh] For 2D: output[h, w] = sum_{dh, dw} input[h - dh, w - dw] * weight[dh, dw] This matches what I had, and the weight is indexed directly by [dh, dw] without spatial flipping. Now for the kernel. Let me finalize the design. Actually, I realize I should also consider whether I can just use CUTLASS or cuDNN through PyTorch APIs but that's not custom CUDA. I need a custom kernel. Let me also consider using a GEMM-based approach. For conv_transpose2d with stride=1, padding=0: 1. The output size is H_out = H_in + K_h - 1, W_out = W_in + K_w - 1 2. We can view each input channel as contributing a (K_h * K_w) -sized "spread" to each spatial output location 3. Or we can use im2col on the input: for each spatial position (h, w), create a column of size (C_in * K_h * K_w) 4. Then do GEMM: (C_out) × (C_in * K_h * K_w) times the im2col matrix. But im2col for conv_transpose2d has a special structure. Actually, we can note that: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] If we define for each output spatial position (h, w), a vector: im2col[h, w, c_in, dh, dw] = input[n, c_in, h-dh, w-dw] (with zero padding outside bounds) Then output[n, :, h, w] = W^T * im2col[h, w, :] where W has shape (C_in*K_h*K_w, C_out). So we can do a GEMM: output_matrix GEMM output = weight_matrix^T × im2col_matrix where weight_matrix has shape (C_out, C_in*K_h*K_w) [transposed view] and im2col_matrix has shape (C_in*K_h*K_w, N*H_out*W_out) and output has shape (C_out, N*H_out*W_out) This is a standard GEMM, but the im2col step involves data movement. Actually, a common pattern is to iterate linearly over all elements to load: ```cuda int total_input = H_load * W_load * TileC_in; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_input; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y * blockDim.z) { int c = idx % TileC_in; int tmp = idx / TileC_in; int w = tmp % W_load; int h = tmp / W_load; // compute global coordinates and load } ``` This gives coalesced memory access if threads with adjacent `tid` load adjacent memory. Since tid has threadIdx.x (W) as the fastest varying component, and we're also iterating with W or C_in as the fastest varying in the linear index... OK here's my kernel draft: ```cuda template<int K_h, int K_w> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TileH = 8; const int TileW = 16; const int TileC_out = 4; const int TileC_in = 8; int n = blockIdx.x; int c_out_b = blockIdx.y; int spatial_b = blockIdx.z; int W_tiles = (W_out + TileW - 1) / TileW; int h_b = spatial_b / W_tiles; int w_b = spatial_b % W_tiles; int tw = threadIdx.x; // 0..TileW-1 int th = threadIdx.y; // 0..TileH-1 int tc = threadIdx.z; // 0..TileC_out-1 int w_out = w_b * TileW + tw; int h_out = h_b * TileH + th; int c_out = c_out_b * TileC_out + tc; if (c_out >= C_out) return; int h_in_start = h_b * TileH - (K_h - 1); int w_in_start = w_b * TileW - (K_w - 1); const int H_load = TileH + K_h - 1; const int W_load = TileW + K_w - 1; __shared__ float smem_input[H_load][W_load][TileC_in]; float acc = 0.0f; // Loop over C_in tiles for (int c_in_b = 0; c_in_b < C_in; c_in_b += TileC_in) { // Load input tile into shared memory int total = H_load * W_load * TileC_in; int tid = tc * (TileH * TileW) + th * TileW + tw; for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += TileH * TileW * TileC_out) { int c = idx % TileC_in; tmp = idx / TileC_in; int w = tmp % W_load; int h = tmp / W_load; int c_in = c_in_b + c; int h_in = h_in_start + h; int w_in = w_in_start + w; float val = 0.0f; if (c_in < C_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; } smem_input[h][w][c] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial sum // For output (h_out, w_out, c_out): // acc += sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[h_out - dh][w_out - dw][c_in] * weight[c_in][c_out][dh][dw] // But we need to map (h_out - dh, w_out - dw) to shared memory indices. // h_in_start = h_b * TileH - (K_h - 1) // h_smem = (h_out - dh) - h_in_start = h_b*TileH + th - dh - (h_b*TileH - K_h + 1) = th - dh + K_h - 1 // w_smem = tw - dw + K_w - 1 // This is in range [0, H_load-1] and [0, W_load-1] by construction. if (h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out) { #pragma unroll for (int dh = 0; dh < K_h; ++dh) { int h_idx = th - dh + (K_h - 1); #pragma unroll for (int dw = 0; dw < K_w; ++dw) { int w_idx = tw - dw + (K_w - 1); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < TileC_in && c_in_b + c < C_in; ++c) { float in_val = smem_input[h_idx][w_idx][c]; // Weight: [C_in, C_out, K_h, K_w] int c_in = c_in_b + c; float w_val = weight[(((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw)]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output if (h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out && c_out < C_out) { output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = acc; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with the weight indexing. Next 220 are c=1, etc. With 1024 threads: - Threads 0-219 load c=0, h=0..9, w=0..21 - Threads 220-439 load c=1, h=0..9, w=0..21 - Threads 440-659 load c=2 - Threads 660-879 load c=3 - Threads 880-1023 load c=4, h=0..6, w=0..21 (partial) - Then in the next iteration, tid starts from 1024: covers the rest of c=4 and c=5, c=6, c=7. Wait, total is 1760. 1760 - 1024 = 736. So second iteration handles 736 elements: c=4 (remaining 144), c=5 (220), c=6 (220), c=7 (220) = 144+660=804? Wait 144+220+220+220 = 804, but we only need 736. Anyway, this is fine. The key question is memory coalescing. Looking at threads in a warp (32 threads): In the first iteration, threads 0-31 load elements 0-31. Elements 0-21: c=0, h=0, w=0..21 (22 elements) Elements 22-31: c=0, h=1, w=0..9 (10 elements) In memory: - c=0, h=0, w=0..21 are contiguous (22 floats = 88 bytes, spans 2-3 cache lines). - c=0, h=1, w=0..9 are also contiguous but at offset W_in = 512 floats from the previous row. So threads 0-21 load contiguous memory (good). Threads 22-31 load memory that's 512 floats away from threads 21's data. This means the warp needs to access two cache lines that are far apart. This is not ideal, but modern GPUs can handle a moderate number of memory transactions per warp. The worst case is 2 transactions for 88 bytes and then another for 40 bytes, so maybe 3 cache lines per warp. Not terrible. And since shared memory load only happens ~7 times per block (1760 elements / 1024 threads = ~2 iterations), it's a small fraction of total time. Actually, I can improve coalescing by having each warp load contiguous memory across the W dimension. Since W is contiguous in memory, if I ensure that threads in a warp load the same (c, h) and different w, they'll be perfectly coalesced. With 1024 threads and 32 threads per warp, there are 32 warps. If I assign warp 0 to load c=0, h=0, w=0..31? But W_load=22. So warp 0 loads c=0, h=0, w=0..21 (22 elements) and then c=0, h=1, w=0..9 (10 elements). Still spans 2 rows. One optimization: since W_in=512 which is >> W_load=22, and memory access is aligned, even strided access within a warp is OK if it fits in a few cache lines. I think I'm overthinking the shared memory loading. Let's just proceed. Now, let me consider using `float` shared memory without bank conflicts. For `smem_input[H_load][W_load][TileC_in]`, with TileC_in=8, if threads in a warp access the same `c` but different `w`, that's a broadcast in the `c` dimension and strided in `w`, which means different banks for different `w` (since bank = address / 4 % 32). For W_load=22 and contiguous `w`, threads in a warp access 32 consecutive banks if they read 32 consecutive `w` values. But our W dimension is only 22. So a warp reading 32 elements would wrap around. With 32 banks, consecutive threads accessing consecutive floats should have no bank conflicts (each thread hits a different bank). But threads in a warp differ in `threadIdx.x` (W dimension), and we access `smem[h][w][c]` where `w = threadIdx.x` or similar. If adjacent threads read adjacent `w` for the same `h` and `c`, they're reading different banks: bank = (h*W_load*8 + w*8 + c) * 4 / 4 % 32 = (h*W_load*8 + w*8 + c) % 32. With w differing by 1, banks differ by 8 (mod 32). So threads 0,1,2,3 read banks 0,8,16,24, threads 4,5,6,7 read banks 0,8,16,24 again! That's a 4-way bank conflict! Oh no, with TileC_in=8, the stride in shared memory between consecutive W values is 8 floats = 32 bytes. Bank = byte_address / 4 % 32 = (w * 32) / 4 % 32 = w * 8 % 32. So banks cycle: 0, 8, 16, 24, 0, 8, 16, 24, ... for w=0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7. This means 4 threads in a warp reading consecutive w values hit the same bank! 4-way bank conflict! To fix this, I should pad the shared memory: `smem_input[H_load][W_load][TileC_in + 1]` or pad between W rows. Actually, the bank for address `&smem[h][w][c]`: - Base offset = (h * W_load + w) * (TileC_in + pad) + c - In bytes: offset * 4 - Bank = (offset * 4 / 4) % 32 = offset % 32 - offset = (h * W_load + w) * (TileC_in + pad) + c - For threads reading same h, c but different w: offset differs by (TileC_in + pad). - We want (TileC_in + pad) % 32 to be odd (or at least coprime with 32) to avoid bank conflicts. - With TileC_in=8, if pad=1, then stride = 9. 9 and 32 are coprime. So banks cycle through all 32 banks! Yes! Just add 1 to the inner dimension: `smem_input[H_load][W_load][TileC_in + 1]`. Then for consecutive w, offset increases by 9, and bank increases by 9 mod 32. Since gcd(9,32)=1, we get no bank conflicts for up to 32 threads! Great. I'll use `TileC_in_padded = TileC_in + 1` in shared memory. Similarly for weight shared memory (if I use it): `smem_weight[K_h][K_w][TileC_out][TileC_in + 1]`. Consecutive threads reading different c_out (with same c_in, dh, dw): stride is TileC_in+1 = 9. Again no bank conflicts. Wait, but if threads read different c_in with same c_out, dh, dw: the accesses are at offset differences of (TileC_out * (TileC_in+1)), which might cause conflicts. But in my current design, I'm not putting weight in shared memory anyway. OK so for input shared memory, use padding. Let me update the kernel. Actually, since I'm not using shared memory for weight, I only need to pad the input shared memory. One more consideration: if I DON'T use shared memory for weight, I can use registers to cache the weight for each (c_in, dh, dw) across the c_out loop. Actually, since each thread computes one c_out and loops over c_in, dh, dw, it reads weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] sequentially. This is already efficient. Wait, I can further optimize by restructuring the compute loop to reduce weight reads: Currently: ```cuda for c_in in c_in_tile: for dh: for dw: in_val = smem[...][c_in] w_val = weight[...][c_out] acc += in_val * w_val ``` Since different threads in a warp (differing in tc) read weight for different c_out but same (c_in, dh, dw), these reads are strided by C_out*K_h*K_w. I could restructure: ```cuda for dh: for dw: for c_in in c_in_tile: in_val = smem[...][c_in] for tc_local in 0..TileC_out-1 (unrolled): c_out = c_out_b + tc_local w_val = weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] acc[tc_local] += in_val * w_val ``` But this requires each thread to compute multiple c_out values. Currently each thread computes one c_out. If I have each thread compute multiple c_out, I need fewer threads or the block computes more outputs. Actually, if each thread computes all TileC_out output channels for one spatial position, the block would only need TileH*TileW threads. For TileH=8, TileW=16, that's 128 threads. Then TileC_out can be larger (like 64), and each thread maintains 64 accumulators in registers. Wait, this is interesting! Let's say: - Block = (TileW, TileH) = (16, 8) = 128 threads - Each thread computes 1 spatial position for ALL C_out channels (64) - Loop over C_in tiles of TileC_in=8: - Load input for TileC_in channels - Each thread computes: for c_out=0..63, for dh=0..2, dw=0..6: acc[c_out] += input[h-dh][w-dw][c_in] * weight[c_in][c_out][dh][dw] Register usage: 64 floats for accumulators. Plus loop variables. Total maybe 80 registers per thread. 128 threads * 80 registers = 10K registers per block. H100 has 64K, so max 6 blocks per SM. But 128 threads * 6 = 768 threads, less than max 2048. So occupancy limited by registers to 768/2048 = 37.5%. Not great. Each thread reads weight for all c_out. For a given (c_in, dh, dw), weight values for c_out=0..63 are spaced by K_h*K_w in memory. Reading 64 strided values per thread is 64 memory accesses. But different threads in a warp read the same weight values (since weight depends on c_in, dh, dw, c_out, not on spatial position). So all 128 threads in a block read the same weight[c_in, 0..63, dh, dw]! With 128 threads = 4 warps, each warp reads weight[c_in, 0..63, dh, dw]. Since c_out varies but each warp reads the same set, L1 can cache these 64 floats and serve all warps. Efficiency depends on the stride: 64 floats at stride 21 = every 21st float. This spans 64*21 = 1344 floats = 5.4KB. That's many cache lines. L1 on H100 is 256KB per SM, so it should fit, but the memory transaction pattern isn't ideal. Wait actually, for fixed c_in, dh, dw: weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] for c_out=0..63: Memory addresses: base + c_out * K_h * K_w + dh * K_w + dw = base + c_out * 21 + offset These are at offsets 0, 21, 42, 63, ... from base. Every 21 floats. There's no way to load these with a single memory transaction. Each load might need its own transaction unless the cache line happens to contain multiple needed values. Cache line = 128 bytes = 32 floats. At offsets 0, 21, 42, 63, 84, 105, 126, 147... - offset 0 is in cache line [0, 31] - offset 21 is also in cache line [0, 31]! - offset 42 is in cache line [32, 63] - offset 63 is in cache line [32, 63] - offset 84 is in cache line [64, 95] - offset 105 is in cache line [96, 127] — wait, 105 is in [96, 127] - offset 126 is in [96, 127] - offset 147 is in [128, 159] So actually, consecutive c_out values pair up into cache lines! c_out=0,1 share a cache line. c_out=2,3 share a cache line. etc. So 64 c_out values require 32 cache lines = 4KB. A warp reading all 64 values needs 32 memory transactions (assuming one load per pair, or 64 individual loads). With 4 warps, same 32 cache lines are read 4 times. With L1 broadcast/hit, maybe 32 transactions per block per (c_in, dh, dw). For a block, per C_in tile: 8 c_in * 3 dh * 7 dw = 168 (c_in,dh,dw) combinations. Each needs 64 weight values. Total ~168 * 2 = 336 transactions. Much better! So the design with smaller TileC_out and shared/weight broadcast is significantly better for weight bandwidth. For general stride, padding, output_padding: - First, the relationship between input and output spatial dimensions: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + K_h + output_padding W_out similarly - For each output position (h_out, w_out): h_out ranges from 0 to H_out-1 We need to find input positions that contribute. The ConvTranspose2d formula (for stride > 1) involves effectively dilating the input by inserting stride-1 zeros, then convolving. Equivalently: output[h_out, w_out] += sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where: h_in = (h_out + padding - dh) / stride w_in = (w_out + padding - dw) / stride and we only include terms where (h_out + padding - dh) % stride == 0, (w_out + padding - dw) % stride == 0, and h_in, w_in are valid. This is a bit more complex to tile because the mapping from output to input is many-to-one (multiple output pixels might map to the same input pixel if stride=1, but for stride>1, some output pixels map to no input pixels at all). For our specific case with stride=1, every output pixel maps to up to 3*7 input pixels. To keep things simple, I'll implement a general kernel that handles all these cases. Since the test uses stride=1, the general kernel won't have branches taken for the specific case. Actually, for performance, I should have two code paths: a fast path for stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, and a general path for other cases. The fast path uses the tiled kernel, the general path uses a simpler direct loop. But wait, the general path only needs to be correct, not fast, since it won't be tested for performance. And I can avoid PyTorch API calls entirely. Let me write a general direct kernel that works for all valid parameters: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose2d_general( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out, int K_h, int K_w, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode idx to n, c_out, h_out, w_out int w_out = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h_out = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int n = tmp / C_out; float acc = 0.0f; int c_out_per_group = C_out / groups; int c_in_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_out_per_group; for (int c = 0; c < c_in_per_group; ++c) { int c_in = g * c_in_per_group + c; for (int dh = 0; dh < K_h; ++dh) { for (int dw = 0; dw < K_w; ++dw) { int h_in = (h_out + pad_h - dh) / stride_h; int w_in = (w_out + pad_w - dw) / stride_w; if ((h_out + pad_h - dh) % stride_h == 0 && (w_out + pad_w - dw) % stride_w == 0 && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { float in_val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((g * c_in_per_group + c) * C_out/groups + (c_out % c_out_per_group)) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } } if (has_bias) { acc += bias[c_out]; } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = acc; } ``` This is a very simple thread-per-output-element kernel. It won't be fast for the large case, but it's correct and handles everything. For the fast path, I'll use the tiled kernel. In ModelNew.forward: ```python if stride == 1 and padding == 0 and output_padding == 0 and groups == 1 and not bias: # Launch fast kernel else: # Launch general kernel ``` This way I avoid PyTorch API calls entirely and handle all cases. One final optimization thought: for the specific case of K_h=3, K_w=7, I can write the inner loop as: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int dh = 0; dh < 3; ++dh) { #pragma unroll for (int dw = 0; dw < 7; ++dw) { ... } } ``` This gives the compiler maximum unrolling freedom. OK, I'll write the code now. Let me structure the Python file: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ ... CUDA code ... """ cpp_source = """ ... C++ wrapper ... """ conv_transpose2d_fast = load_inline( name="conv_transpose2d_fast", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["conv_transpose2d_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, groups=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size if isinstance(kernel_size, tuple) else (kernel_size, kernel_size) self.stride = stride if isinstance(stride, tuple) else (stride, stride) self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding) self.output_padding = output_padding if isinstance(output_padding, tuple) else (output_padding, output_padding) self.groups = groups self.has_bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, out_channels // groups, *self.kernel_size)) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) if bias else None # Initialize to match PyTorch default nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=5**0.5) if self.bias is not None: nn.init.zeros_(self.bias) def forward(self, x): # Check if we can use fast path if (self.stride == (1, 1) and self.padding == (0, 0) and self.output_padding == (0, 0) and self.groups == 1 and not self.has_bias): return conv_transpose2d_fast.conv_transpose2d_forward( x, self.weight, self.in_channels, self.out_channels, self.kernel_size[0], self.kernel_size[1] ) else: # General case - but since we don't expect this in tests, # and to avoid using PyTorch's API which might be flagged, # we can still use a generic CUDA kernel or raise error ... ``` Wait, but if Model is initialized with `nn.ConvTranspose2d`, its weight and bias have a specific shape and initialization. Let me draft the full Python code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // Fast kernel for stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, groups=1 template<int K_h, int K_w> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TileH = 8; const int TileW = 16; const int TileC_out = 4; const int TileC_in = 16; const int C_in_pad = TileC_in + 1; int n = blockIdx.x; int c_out_b = blockIdx.y; int spatial_b = blockIdx.z; int W_tiles = (W_out + TileW - 1) / TileW; int h_b = spatial_b / W_tiles; int w_b = spatial_b % W_tiles; int tw = threadIdx.x; int th = threadIdx.y; int tc = threadIdx.z; int w_out = w_b * TileW + tw; int h_out = h_b * TileH + th; int c_out = c_out_b * TileC_out + tc; if (c_out >= C_out) return; int h_in_start = h_b * TileH - (K_h - 1); int w_in_start = w_b * TileW - (K_w - 1); const int H_load = TileH + K_h - 1; const int W_load = TileW + K_w - 1; __shared__ float smem_input[H_load * W_load * C_in_pad]; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in_b = 0; c_in_b < C_in; c_in_b += TileC_in) { int total_load = H_load * W_load * TileC_in; int tid = tc * (TileH * TileW) + th * TileW + tw; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_load; idx += TileH * TileW * TileC_out) { int c = idx % TileC_in; int tmp = idx / TileC_in; int w = tmp % W_load; int h = tmp / W_load; int c_in = c_in_b + c; int h_in = h_in_start + h; int w_in = w_in_start + w; float val = 0.0f; if (c_in < C_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; } smem_input[h * W_load * C_in_pad + w * C_in_pad + c] = val; if (h_out >= H_out - out_pad_h || w_out >= W_out - out_pad_w) { output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f; return; } float acc = has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f; int c_out_per_group = C_out / groups; int c_in_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / c_out_per_group; for (int c = 0; c < c_in_per_group; ++c) { int c_in = g * c_in_per_group + c; for (int dh = 0; dh < K_h; ++dh) { for (int dw = 0; dw < K_w; ++dw) { int h_in = (h_out + pad_h - dh); int w_in = (w_out + pad_w - dw); if (h_in % stride_h == 0 && w_in % stride_w == 0) { h_in = h_in / stride_h; w_in = w_in / stride_w; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { float in_val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[((((g * c_in_per_group + c) * c_out_per_group + (c_out % c_out_per_group)) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw)]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } } } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = acc; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int in_channels, int out_channels, int kernel_h, int kernel_w, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int N = input.size(0); int H_in = input.size(2); int W_in = input.size(3); int H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride_h - 2 * pad_h + kernel_h + out_pad_h; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride_w - 2 * pad_w + kernel_w + out_pad_w; auto output = torch::empty({N, out_channels, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); if (stride_h == 1 && stride_w == 1 && pad_h == 0 && pad_w == 0 && out_pad_h == 0 && out_pad_w == 0 && groups == 1 && !has_bias) { const int TileH = 8; const int TileW = 16; const int TileC_out = 4; dim3 block(TileW, TileH, TileC_out); int W_tiles = (W_out + TileW - 1) / TileW; int H_tiles = (H_out + TileH - 1) / TileH; int spatial_tiles = W_tiles * H_tiles; dim3 grid(N, (out_channels + TileC_out - 1) / TileC_out, spatial_tiles); if (kernel_h == 3 && kernel_w == 7) { conv_transpose2d_fast_kernel<3, 7><<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, in_channels, out_channels, H_in, W_in, H_out, W_out ); } else { // Could add more template instantiations, but for now use general path // Or maybe we can use a runtime-param version // For now fall through to general goto general_path; } } else { general_path: int total = N * out_channels * H_out * W_out; } return output; } """ conv_transpose2d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv_transpose2d_cuda", cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["conv_transpose2d_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, groups=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size if isinstance(kernel_size, tuple) else (kernel_size, kernel_size) self.stride = stride if isinstance(stride, tuple) else (stride, stride) self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding) self.output_padding = output_padding if isinstance(output_padding, tuple) else (output_padding, output_padding) self.groups = groups self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, out_channels // groups, *self.kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=5**0.5) if bias: nn.init.zeros_(self.bias) def forward(self, x): x = x.contiguous() weight = self.weight.contiguous() bias = self.bias.contiguous() if self.bias is not None else None return conv_transpose2d_cuda.conv_transpose2d_forward( x, weight, bias, self.in_channels, self.out_channels, self.kernel_size[0], self.kernel_size[1], self.stride[0], self.stride[1], self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.output_padding[0], self.output_padding[1], self.groups, self.bias is not None ) ``` Wait, `goto` in C++ is valid but not great style. For conv_transpose2d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, groups=1: PyTorch docs say: "output[N, C_out, H_out, W_out] += bias[C_out] + sum_{k=0}^{C_in-1} weight[C_out, k, s, t] * input[N, k, H_out + s - pad_h, W_out + t - pad_w]" Wait, PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d formula from the docs: ``` H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 ``` For dilation=1, stride=1, padding=0: H_out = H_in - 1 + kernel_size - 1 + 1 = H_in + kernel_size - 1. From PyTorch source/docs: For ConvTranspose2d, the forward pass computes: ``` output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, h', w'] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] ``` where the exact formula for h' depends on stride, padding, etc. Actually, for groups=1, the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, K_h, K_w). And the operation is the transpose of conv2d. If conv2d computes: ``` y[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} x[n, c_in, h + dh, w + dw] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] ``` (with padding, stride, etc.) Then conv_transpose2d computes: ``` z[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, dh, dw} y[n, c_out, h - dh, w - dw] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] ``` So with our notation where `input` is `y` and `output` is `z`: ``` output[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out, dh, dw} input[n, c_out, h - dh, w - dw] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] ``` Wait, this says output channels are indexed by `c_in`! That's because in the transpose, the input and output channel dimensions are swapped. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d has `in_channels` and `out_channels` parameters. The output has `out_channels` channels. The weight is `(in_channels, out_channels/groups, K_h, K_w)`. So the actual formula is: ``` output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, h - dh - pad_h, w - dw - pad_w] / stride ... ``` Hmm, I need to be more careful. But first, let me think about the weight layout one more time. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d stores weight as (in_channels, out_channels, *kernel_size). For the operation to be the transpose of Conv2d: Conv2d: y = A * x where weight defines A ConvTranspose2d: z = A^T * y If Conv2d uses weight W[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] to compute cross-correlation: y[c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh,dw} x[c_in, h+dh, w+dw] * W[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] Then for ConvTranspose2d, the output should be: z[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out} sum_{dh,dw} y[n, c_out, h-dh, w-dw] * W[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] But PyTorch calls this output dimension "out_channels" even though it's really the transposed input dimension. So the weight in ConvTranspose2d is stored with shape (in_channels, out_channels, *kernel_size), where "in_channels" here corresponds to the c_out dimension in the formula above, and "out_channels" corresponds to c_in. Wait, that means in my kernel, the weight should be indexed as: weight[c_out_from_input, c_out_of_output, dh, dw] where c_out_from_input is really the channel dimension of the ConvTranspose2d input, and c_out_of_output is the channel dimension of the ConvTranspose2d output. For my fast kernel, I used: ```cuda float w_val = weight[(((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw)]; ``` This assumes weight is stored as [C_in, C_out, K_h, K_w] with K_w being the innermost dimension. Since PyTorch is row-major, the strides are: - stride for C_in: C_out * K_h * K_w - stride for C_out: K_h * K_w - stride for K_h: K_w - stride for K_w: 1 So `weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw]` is at index `c_in * C_out * K_h * K_w + c_out * K_h * K_w + dh * K_w + dw`. This matches my formula. Great. And my input indexing: ```cuda input[((n * C_in + c_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in] ``` This assumes [N, C, H, W] row-major with W innermost. Correct. And output: ```cuda output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] ``` Correct. And the formula: output[n, c_out, h_out, w_out] += sum_{c_in, dh, dw} input[n, c_in, h_out - dh, w_out - dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] For valid input indices only. So for input [a] (N=1, C=1, H=1): output[0] = a * w0 (dh=0) output[1] = a * w1 (dh=1) Formula: output[h] = sum_{dh} input[h - dh] * w[dh] For h=0: dh=0, input[0]*w[0] = a*w0 For h=1: dh=1, input[0]*w[1] = a*w1 Yes! In the compute loop: ```cuda float in_val = smem_input[h_idx * W_load * C_in_pad + w_idx * C_in_pad + c]; ``` For a given iteration `c`, threads in a warp have different `h_idx` and `w_idx`. - `h_idx = th - dh + (K_h - 1)` where `th = threadIdx.y`. - `w_idx = tw - dw + (K_w - 1)` where `tw = threadIdx.x`. In a warp of 32 threads (for block layout (16, 8, 4)): If a warp consists of threads with tc=0, th=0..1, tw=0..15: - For fixed dh, dw: h_idx is 0-dh+2=2-dh or 1-dh+2=3-dh. w_idx = tw-dw+6. - tw ranges 0..15, so w_idx ranges 6-dw to 21-dw. - Bank = (h_idx * W_load * C_in_pad + w_idx * C_in_pad + c) % 32. - With C_in_pad=16: bank = (h_idx * 22 * 16 + w_idx * 16 + c) % 32. - h_idx * 352 % 32 = h_idx * (11 * 32) % 32 = 0. - w_idx * 16 % 32 = (w_idx % 2) * 16. - c % 32. - Bank = ((w_idx % 2) * 16 + c) % 32. So for threads with even w_idx: bank = c % 32. For threads with odd w_idx: bank = (16 + c) % 32. Half the warp (16 threads with even w_idx) reads banks c, c, ..., c. The other half (16 threads with odd w_idx) reads banks 16+c, 16+c, ..., 16+c. Since half the warp reads the same bank (c), that's a 16-way bank conflict! OH NO! This is terrible! With C_in_pad=16, we have 16-way bank conflicts during compute! With C_in_pad=17: bank = (h_idx * 22 * 17 + w_idx * 17 + c) % 32. 22*17 = 374. 374 % 32 = 374 - 11*32 = 374 - 352 = 22. 17 % 32 = 17. Bank = (h_idx * 22 + w_idx * 17 + c) % 32. For h_idx=2, w_idx=6 to 21: bank = (44 + w_idx*17 + c) % 32. w_idx=6: bank = (44 + 102 + c) % 32 = (146 + c) % 32 = (18 + c) % 32. w_idx=7: bank = (18 + 17 + c) % 32 = (35 + c) % 32 = (3 + c) % 32. w_idx=8: bank = (3 + 17 + c) % 32 = (20 + c) % 32. For c fixed (all threads reading same c in same loop iteration), banks for w_idx=6,7,8,9,... are 18+c, 3+c, 20+c, 5+c, 22+c, 7+c, 24+c, 9+c, 26+c, 11+c, 28+c, 13+c, 30+c, 15+c, 0+c, 1+c. Wait, these are all different modulo 32! Let me verify: w_idx=6: 18 w_idx=7: 3 w_idx=8: 20 w_idx=9: 5 w_idx=10: 22 w_idx=11: 7 w_idx=12: 24 w_idx=13: 9 w_idx=14: 26 w_idx=15: 11 w_idx=16: 28 w_idx=17: 13 w_idx=18: 30 w_idx=19: 15 w_idx=20: 0 w_idx=21: 1 All 16 values: 18, 3, 20, 5, 22, 7, 24, 9, 26, 11, 28, 13, 30, 15, 0, 1. Are these all distinct modulo 32? Yes! None repeat. So with C_in_pad=17, for a fixed c and h_idx, 16 threads reading 16 different w_idx values hit 16 different banks! No bank conflict! Bank = (22*h_idx + 17*w_idx + c) % 32. For two different (h1, w1) and (h2, w2) to have the same bank: 22*(h1-h2) + 17*(w1-w2) ≡ 0 (mod 32). Since gcd(22, 17, 32) = gcd(gcd(22,32), 17) = gcd(2, 17) = 1. There will be some pairs that satisfy this. For example: 22*1 + 17*(-1) = 5 ≠ 0 (mod 32). Let me draft the code carefully. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> template<int K_h, int K_w> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TileH = 8; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int in_channels, int out_channels, int kernel_h, int kernel_w, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int N = input.size(0); Let me finalize the code and try compiling. Oh wait, I realize there's a subtle bug in my fast kernel. The `c` loop: ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < TileC_in && c_in_b + c < C_in; ++c) ``` Since `TileC_in` is 16 and `C_in` might not be a multiple of 16, the last tile might have fewer valid iterations. With `C_in=64`, there are exactly 4 tiles, each with 16 iterations. But what if C_in=63? The last tile would have c_in_b=48, and c=0..14 valid, c=15 invalid. The condition `c_in_b + c < C_in` handles this. In the compute loop, the condition is checked per iteration. The compiler can't fully unroll because of the runtime check, but with `#pragma unroll`, it should still unroll and skip the last iteration. But actually, since `c_in_b + c < C_in` is known to be true for the first 3 tiles and partially true for the last, the compiler might generate decent code. For our test case with C_in=64, it's always true, so the compiler might optimize away the check in practice if it inlines properly. Now let me think about one more potential issue: the general kernel uses `int total = N * C_out * H_out * W_out`. For large tensors, this might overflow `int` (max 2^31-1 ≈ 2.1B). For our sizes: 8 * 64 * 514 * 518 = 136,511,488, which fits in int. But for larger sizes, it could overflow. I'll use `int64_t` for safety. Actually, for the tested case, int is fine. I'll use `int64_t` anyway. Let me write the complete code now. Actually, I want to make one more change for potential performance improvement. In the fast kernel, the weight access pattern is: ```cuda float w_val = weight[(((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw)]; ``` For fixed c_in, c_out, and varying dh, dw in the unrolled loops, the address changes by K_w or 1. These are contiguous or near-contiguous memory accesses. For a fixed thread and fixed c_in, the weight reads for different dh and dw are sequential in memory. This is excellent cache locality. Actually wait, for fixed c_in and c_out, weight is [K_h][K_w], so consecutive dw values are contiguous. For dh incrementing, the jump is K_w. So within the dw loop, reads are perfectly contiguous. Between dh values, there's a small stride of K_w=7. This is very cache-friendly. And since each thread reads the weight for its own c_out, and threads in a warp might have different c_out values, the reads are strided by C_out*K_h*K_w. But as I analyzed, L1 can handle this. One more thing: I should make sure the compiler uses `__ldg` or similar for read-only global memory. The `__restrict__` qualifier helps, and the compiler might automatically use LDG instructions. I can also explicitly use `__ldg()` if needed, but `__restrict__` is usually sufficient. Wait, for compute capability >= 3.5, `__restrict__` on const pointers lets the compiler use the read-only data cache (texture/LDG). H100 definitely supports this. OK, I'll trust the compiler. Let me write the final code now. I'll be very careful with syntax.

reasoning
The issue is that the max difference is ~0.0005 which is above the fp32 tolerance of 1e-4. This is likely due to: 1. Different order of accumulation causing floating point differences 2. A bug in my fast kernel logic The differences are very small (0.0005 max, 0.00007 avg), which suggests it's just numerical precision from different accumulation ordering. But since the tolerance is strict, I need to match PyTorch's output more closely. One approach: instead of a custom fast kernel, use matrix multiplication via im2col which PyTorch itself uses. But even that might have different ordering. Let me think about the actual conv transpose math: - In conv2d transpose with stride 1, padding 0, it's essentially a full convolution (not cross-correlation) of the input with the weight - Each weight K_h x K_w contributes to output_h + dh, output_w + dw for dh,dw in [0, K_h-1], [0, K_w-1] - Wait no, conv transpose with stride=1, padding=0, no output padding is exactly equivalent to conv2d with the same weight but treating 180-degree rotated weight Actually, for stride=1, padding=0: - conv2d_transpose(input, weight) = conv2d(padded_input, weight_rotated_180) - But with padding=0, there's no padding needed - Actually, conv2d_transpose with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to conv2d with weight flipped both H and W Let me verify: For conv_transpose2d: - output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum(c_in, i, j) input[n, c_in, h+i-pad_top, w+j-pad_left] * weight[c_in, c_out, K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j] - With stride=1, padding=0: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum(c_in, i, j) input[n, c_in, h+i, w+j] * weight[c_in, c_out, K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j] for h+i in [0, H-1], w+j in [0, W-1] So h in [-(K_h-1), H-1], w in [-(K_w-1), W-1] - But actually output size = (H-1)*1 - 0 + K_h = H + K_h - 1 - So h in [0, H+K_h-2], w in [0, W+K_w-2] - For valid h,w: we need h+i >= 0 and h+i < H, so i >= -h and i < H-h But i in [0, K_h-1], so i >= max(0, -h) and i < min(K_h, H-h) My kernel had: h_idx = th - dh + (K_h - 1). Then I read from shared memory at h_idx. For output h_out = th, we want to access all input positions h_in such that dh is a valid offset. In conv transpose, output[h_out] gets contribution from input at position h_out + dh - (K_h-1), with dh in [0, K_h-1]. So h_in = h_out + dh - (K_h-1) = th + dh - (K_h-1). In shared memory, I loaded the tile starting at h_in_start = h_b * TileH - (K_h - 1). So the element h_in is at position h_in - h_in_start = th + dh - (K_h-1) - h_in_start = th + dh - (K_h-1) - (h_b*TileH - (K_h-1)) = th + dh - h_b*TileH = (h_out - h_b * TileH) + dh = th + dh. Wait, h_idx = th - dh + (K_h - 1). Let me recheck. For tile starting at h_in_start = h_b*TileH - (K_h-1), the loaded index for h_in is: h_local = h_in - h_in_start = h_in - h_b*TileH + (K_h-1) For output position h_out = h_b*TileH + th, the input accessed is h_in = h_out + dh - (K_h-1) for dh in [0, K_h-1]. h_local = h_out + dh - (K_h-1) - h_b*TileH + (K_h-1) = th + dh. So the correct index in shared memory is (th + dh), not (th - dh + (K_h - 1)). Let me fix this indexing bug. This is why the output is wrong. Actually wait. Let me re-derive more carefully. For conv transpose with stride=1, padding=0: output[h, w] = sum(c_in, c_out_of_group, dh, dw) input[h + dh, w + dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, K_h - 1 - dh, K_w - 1 - dw] where the sum is over valid dh, dw such that h+dh in [0, H_in-1], w+dw in [0, W_in-1]. So for output h_out, we need: dh in [max(0, -h_out), min(K_h-1, H_in-1-h_out)] But in my kernel, I loop dh from 0 to K_h-1 and compute: h_idx = th - dh + (K_h - 1) This is equivalent to accessing input at h_in_start + h_idx = h_b*TileH - (K_h-1) + th - dh + (K_h-1) = h_b*TileH + th - dh = h_out - dh. But from the formula, we need input at h_out + dh, not h_out - dh! So I got the sign wrong! The correct index should be h_idx = th + dh for the shared memory. Similarly for width: w_idx = tw + dw. And the weight indexing also changes: weight[c_in, c_out, K_h - 1 - dh, K_w - 1 - dw] = weight[c_in * C_out * K_h * K_w + c_out * K_h * K_w + (K_h-1-dh) * K_w + (K_w-1-dw)] In my kernel I used weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw] which is weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw]. But the conv transpose actually uses the rotated version! Wait, there's a subtlety: PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d uses weight stored as [in_channels, out_channels/groups, K_h, K_w]. The actual operation performed is a transposed convolution. The relationship between the forward convolution and the transpose weights matters. Actually, let me just look at the reference mathematically: For a standard conv2d: output[n,c_out,h,w] = sum(c_in, i, j) input[n, c_in, h*stride+i-pad, w*stride+j-pad] * weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] For conv_transpose2d: output[n,c_out,h,w] = sum(c_in, i, j) input[n, c_in, h+i-pad, w+j-pad] * weight[c_in, c_out, i, j] where the indices are interpreted differently. Actually in PyTorch, the conv_transpose2d weight is [in_channels, out_channels/groups, *kernel_size]. And the math is: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum over k,l,m of input[n, k, stride0*h + m0 - padding0, stride1*w + m1 - padding1] * weight[k, c_out, m0, m1] * (1 if valid else 0) Hmm, actually PyTorch's conv_transpose2d is the gradient of conv2d w.r.t. input. The gradient w.r.t. input for conv2d is: dL/dx[n, c_in, h, w] = sum(c_out, i, j) dL/dy[n, c_out, (h+pad-i)//stride, (w+pad-j)//stride] * weight[c_out, c_out_start:c_out_end, i, j] For conv_transpose2d, the forward is essentially this gradient computation where dL/dy is the input to conv_transpose2d and weight is transposed. In PyTorch, the weight is not actually transposed; it's just indexed differently. Let me be super concrete. For conv2d forward with stride=1, padding=0: y[n, c_out, h, w] = sum(c_in, i, j) x[n, c_in, h+i, w+j] * w[c_out, c_in, i, j] (where i in [0, K_h-1], j in [0, K_w-1]) For conv2d backward w.r.t. inputs: dx[n, c_in, h, w] = sum(c_out, i, j) dy[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * w[c_out, c_in, i, j] = sum(c_out, i, j) dy[n, c_out, h-i, w-j] * w[c_out, c_in, i, j] = sum(c_out, i, j) dy[n, c_out, h+i-K_h+1, w+j-K_w+1] * w[c_out, c_in, K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j] So conv_transpose2d(x, weight) where weight is [in_channels, out_channels, K_h, K_w] computes: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum(c_in, i, j) x[n, c_in, h+i-K_h+1, w+j-K_w+1] * weight[c_in, c_out, K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j] with valid bounds checking. For valid positions: x at [..., h+i-K_h+1, w+j-K_w+1] must be in bounds. So h+i-K_h+1 >= 0 => i >= K_h-1-h h+i-K_h+1 < H_in => i < H_in + K_h - 1 - h And i in [0, K_h-1]. For a given h_out, we have: i in [max(0, K_h-1-h_out), min(K_h-1, H_in+K_h-2-h_out)] and similarly for j. For my fast kernel, shared memory contains input starting at h_in_start = h_b*TileH - (K_h-1). For output at h_out = h_b*TileH + th, the input index is h_in = h_out + i - (K_h-1). In shared memory: h_local = h_in - h_in_start = h_out + i - (K_h-1) - h_b*TileH + (K_h-1) = th + i. So I should use h_idx = th + i (where i corresponds to my dh loop variable), not th - dh + (K_h - 1). And the weight index should be weight[c_in, c_out, K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j]. Let me fix the kernel. Actually, there's a much simpler thing: PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0, no bias, groups=1 computing from [N, C_in, H, W] to [N, C_out, H+K_h-1, W+K_w-1] is just: for each output position (h, w): for dh in [0, K_h-1]: for dw in [0, K_w-1]: if h-dh >= 0 and h-dh < H and w-dw >= 0 and w-dw < W: output[n,c_out,h,w] += input[n, :, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[:, c_out, dh, dw] Wait, is the weight indexing dh or K_h-1-dh? Let me test: Consider a conv_transpose2d from 1 channel to 1 channel with kernel size (3,3), input size (2,2), stride=1, padding=0. Output size = (2-1)*1 - 0 + 3 = 4x4. For conv2d with kernel 3x3, stride 1, padding 0 on a 4x4 input, output is 2x2. The transpose should effectively give us back the input (approximately). Actually, if I run a simple test in PyTorch: ```python conv = nn.Conv2d(1, 1, 3, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False) conv_t = nn.ConvTranspose2d(1, 1, 3, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False) conv_t.weight.data = conv.weight.data.transpose(0, 1) x = torch.randn(1, 1, 4, 4) y = conv(x) z = conv_t(y) ``` This should give z approximately equal to x. The weight relationship means conv_transpose2d uses weight effectively as the transpose of conv2d weight. Let me be very careful. Conv2d forward: y[h, w] = sum(i=0..K_h-1, j=0..K_w-1) x[h+i, w+j] * w[i, j] (with x and w viewed as the appropriate slices). Dx (gradient w.r.t. x) from dy: dx[h, w] = sum(i=0..K_h-1, j=0..K_w-1) dy[h-i, w-j] * w[i, j] For this to be valid, dy must be defined at h-i, w-j. = sum(i=0..K_h-1, j=0..K_w-1) dy[h+K_h-1-i, w+K_w-1-j] * w[K_h-1-i, K_w-1-j] Let i' = K_h-1-i, j' = K_w-1-j: = sum(i'=K_h-1..0, j'=K_w-1..0) dy[h+i', w+j'] * w[i', j'] = sum(i'=0..K_h-1, j'=0..K_w-1) dy[h+i', w+j'] * w[i', j'] (with the understanding that only valid positions contribute) So conv_transpose2d(dy, w) should give us back approximately x (if w is such that the operation is approximately an inverse). No actually, for the general case of conv_transpose2d, PyTorch internally may compute it by first dilating the input (inserting zeros), adding padding around it, and then doing a standard conv2d. For stride=1, there's no dilation needed. So with pad=0, it would add (K-1) padding around the input and do a standard conv2d with the weight. For standard conv2d with weight W and input X padded by (K-1): `out[h, w] = sum_{dh, dw} X_padded[h + dh, w + dw] * W[dh, dw]` where X_padded has padding K-1 on all sides. For h_out = h + pad_h where pad_h = K_h - 1: `out[h_out - (K_h - 1), w_out - (K_w - 1)] = sum_{dh, dw} X[h_out - K_h + 1 + dh, w_out - K_w + 1 + dw] * W[dh, dw]` If we let h_out' = h_out - K_h + 1, then: `out[h_out', w_out'] = sum_{dh, dw} X[h_out' + dh, w_out' + dw] * W[dh, dw]` This is the same as standard conv2d on X. But for the transpose operation, we want: `z[a, b] = sum_{dh, dw} y[a - dh, b - dw] * W[dh, dw]` Is this equivalent to conv2d with padded input? If we pad y by K-1 on all sides to get y_padded, then: `conv2d(y_padded, W)[a, b] = sum_{dh, dw} y_padded[a + dh, b + dw] * W[dh, dw] = sum_{dh, dw} y[a + dh, b + dw] * W[dh, dw]` This is NOT the same as our desired `z[a, b] = sum_{dh, dw} y[a - dh, b - dw] * W[dh, dw]`. BUT, if we flip W: `conv2d(y_padded, flip(W))[a, b] = sum_{dh, dw} y[a + dh, b + dw] * W[K_h - 1 - dh, K_w - 1 - dw]` Let dh' = K_h - 1 - dh, dw' = K_w - 1 - dw: `= sum_{dh', dw'} y[a + K_h - 1 - dh', b + K_w - 1 - dw'] * W[dh', dw']` This gives y_padded[a + K_h - 1 - dh', ...] which corresponds to y[...] only if a + K_h - 1 - dh' is in valid range. For a = 0, dh' ranges 0 to K_h - 1, so index = K_h - 1 - dh' which is in [0, K_h - 1], corresponding to padded region. Actually, `conv2d(pad(y, K-1), flip(W))[a, b] = sum_{dh, dw} y[a + dh, b + dw] * W[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw]`. For this to equal `sum_{dh, dw} y[a - dh, b - dw] * W[dh, dw]`, we need to verify. Let h = a, w = b. The left side at (h, w) is: `sum_{dh=0}^{K-1} sum_{dw=0}^{K-1} y_padded[h + dh, w + dw] * W[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw]` Since y_padded is zero-padded, and for h in [0, H+K-2], w in [0, W+K-2]: - When h + dh corresponds to a valid y index, y_padded[h+dh, w+dw] = y[h+dh - (K_h-1), w+dw - (K_w-1)] if those are valid y indices. - Actually no, if pad is K-1 on all sides, then y_padded[i, j] = y[i - (K_h-1), j - (K_w-1)] when i >= K_h-1 and j >= K_w-1 and i < H+K_h-1, j < W+K_w-1. So for term dh, dw: `y_padded[h + dh, w + dw] * W[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw]` = `y[h + dh - K_h + 1, w + dw - K_w + 1] * W[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw]` when the indices are valid. Let dh' = K_h - 1 - dh, dw' = K_w - 1 - dw. Then dh = K_h - 1 - dh', dw = K_w - 1 - dw'. The y index becomes: `h + K_h - 1 - dh' - K_h + 1 = h - dh'` `w + K_w - 1 - dw' - K_w + 1 = w - dw'` So: `conv2d(pad(y, K-1), flip(W))[h, w] = sum_{dh', dw'} y[h - dh', w - dw'] * W[dh', dw']` This IS exactly the conv_transpose2d! So mathematically: `conv_transpose2d(y, W) = conv2d(pad(y, K-1), flip(W))` But in PyTorch, does ConvTranspose2d internally do this? No, because PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d uses the weight as-is (not flipped). The "transpose" in the name refers to the algebraic transpose of the convolution operator, and the weight is stored in the transposed format already. But wait, if PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d computes the mathematical transpose of conv2d, and conv2d internally uses the weight as-is (with implicit spatial correlation not convolution), then: In PyTorch: - `conv2d(x, w)` computes correlation: `sum x[h+dh, w+dw] * w[dh, dw]` - `conv_transpose2d(x, w)` computes the transpose of the above linear operator. The transpose operation, as we derived, is: `sum y[h-dh, w-dw] * w[dh, dw]`. Wait, but if PyTorch implements this by internally doing `conv2d(pad(y, K-1), flip(w))`, then our weight `w` should be used as-is but PyTorch would flip it internally. Actually no, the key insight is: - The transpose of correlation (with weight w) is not correlation with flip(w), but rather a specific operation. - In signal processing, convolution is `x * h = sum x[n-k] h[k]`. Correlation is `sum x[n+k] h[k]`. - The adjoint of correlation is... let me think. If operator T is: `(Tx)[n] = sum_k x[n+k] w[k]` Then `(T^* y)[n] = sum_k y[n-k] w[k]` (with appropriate range restrictions). If PyTorch's Conv2d computes correlation and ConvTranspose2d computes its adjoint, then: - Conv2d: `y[n] = sum_k x[n+k] w[k]` - ConvTranspose2d: `z[n] = sum_k y[n-k] w[k]` But if PyTorch implements ConvTranspose2d using the convolution primitive (which also computes correlation), it would need to flip the weight. Wait, actually! For transposed convolution with stride=1, pad=0: - The operation can be written as: output = col2im(matmul(im2col_input, weight_reshaped)) - But im2col for stride=1, pad=0 is just the identity (for the spatial positions, the "patches" overlap). Actually, for conv2d with stride=1, pad=0: - im2col produces a matrix of shape (N * H_out * W_out, C_in * K_h * K_w) - Then output = matmul(im2col, weight_flat.T) gives (N * H_out * W_out, C_out) For conv_transpose2d, if we think of it as the transpose of this: - We start with input of shape (N, C_in, H_in, W_in) - We want output of shape (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) where H_out = H_in + K_h - 1. The transpose of (im2col; matmul with W.T) is (matmul with W; col2im). But actually computing this requires: 1. Flatten input to (N * H_in * W_in, C_in) 2. For each spatial position, we need to create a patch that spans K_h * K_w output positions. 3. Multiply by weight of shape (C_in, C_out * K_h * K_w). 4. This gives (N * H_in * W_in, C_out * K_h * K_w). 5. Then sum-aggregate (col2im) into the output. The col2im step sums overlapping patches. Specifically, each output position (h_out, w_out) receives contributions from input positions (h_in, w_in) such that h_out = h_in + dh and w_out = w_in + dw for some dh in [0, K_h-1], dw in [0, K_w-1]. So output[n, c_out, h_out, w_out] = sum_{dh, dw} (input_flat[n, h_in, w_in, :] @ weight[:, c_out, dh, dw]) where h_in = h_out - dh, w_in = w_out - dw. This is exactly my formula! Reshape input to (N * H_in * W_in, C_in) 2. Multiply by weight_reshaped of shape (C_in, C_out * K_h * K_w) to get (N * H_in * W_in, C_out * K_h * K_w) 3. Reshape to (N, H_in, W_in, C_out, K_h, K_w) 4. "Scatter-add" into output of shape (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) This scatter-add operation is where the overlapping happens. Actually, an alternative formulation: 1. For each kernel position (dh, dw): - Weight slice: W_dh_dw of shape (C_in, C_out) - Input slice: input of shape (N, C_in, H_in, W_in) - Compute matmul: output_slice = input.transpose(1, 2).transpose(2, 3) @ W_dh_dw This gives shape (N, H_in, W_in, C_out) - Place output_slice into output[n, c_out, h_in + dh, w_in + dw] If we do this for all (dh, dw), we'd get the correct result. This is essentially computing independent convolutions for each kernel position and shifting the result. Using PyTorch ops: ```python weight_flat = self.weight.permute(2, 3, 0, 1) # (K_h, K_w, C_in, C_out) output = torch.zeros(N, C_out, H_out, W_out, device=x.device) for dh in range(K_h): for dw in range(K_w): # x_slice: (N, C_in, H_in, W_in) # w_slice: (C_in, C_out) out_slice = torch.einsum('nchw,co->nohw', x, weight_flat[dh, dw]) output[:, :, dh:dh+H_in, dw:dw+W_in] += out_slice ``` This would use PyTorch's optimized GEMM (via einsum -> batch matmul) and accumulation. Actually, many resources online discuss how transposed convolution is often implemented using flipped kernels. Let me think about this carefully. In signal processing: - Convolution: `(f * g)[n] = sum f[m] g[n - m]` - Cross-correlation: `(f ⋆ g)[n] = sum f[m] g[n + m]` PyTorch Conv2d computes cross-correlation. The transpose of cross-correlation is... let's see: If `y = x ⋆ w` (cross-correlation), then `y[n] = sum_m x[m] w[n + m]`. For the adjoint/transpose: we want `z` such that `<x, z> = <y, T(x)>` where `T(x) = x ⋆ w`. `<y, T(x)> = sum_n y[n] (sum_m x[m] w[n + m]) = sum_m x[m] (sum_n y[n] w[n + m])` So `z[m] = sum_n y[n] w[n + m]`. Let `k = n + m`, then `n = k - m`: `z[m] = sum_k y[k - m] w[k]` This is a CONVOLUTION (not cross-correlation) of y with w! Because: `(y * w)[m] = sum_k y[k] w[m - k]`... Wait no. Actually, if we define `w'[k] = w[k]`, then `z[m] = sum_k y[k - m] w[k]`. Let `j = k - m`, then `k = j + m`: `z[m] = sum_j y[j] w[j + m]` This is CROSS-CORRELATION between y and w! Wait, so `z[m] = (y ⋆ w)[m]`? No, `z[m] = sum_j y[j] w[j + m]` is exactly cross-correlation! So the transpose of cross-correlation is cross-correlation! (Using the same weight, not flipped.) This confirms my direct formula is correct. PyTorch ConvTranspose2d computes cross-correlation with the same weight. If someone claims it's equivalent to conv2d with flipped weight, they would be confusing correlation and convolution. But since PyTorch's conv2d computes correlation, conv_transpose2d ALSO computes correlation (with the same weight). So my formula is correct. Instead of writing a full custom conv_transpose kernel, what if I write a kernel that converts the operation to a `torch.mm` call? For conv_transpose2d with stride=1, pad=0, we can view it as: 1. For each output position (h_out, w_out), it's a dot product between: - A vector of length C_in * K_h * K_w containing input values from the receptive field - A vector of length C_in * K_h * K_w containing the weight values for output channel c_out. Wait, that's the direct approach. The GEMM approach reorganizes this as: - Create a matrix `A` of shape (N * H_out * W_out, C_in * K_h * K_w) where each row is the receptive field for one output position. - Create a matrix `B` of shape (C_in * K_h * K_w, C_out) containing the weights. - Compute `C = A @ B` of shape (N * H_out * W_out, C_out). - Reshape `C` to (N, H_out, W_out, C_out) and permute. But for conv_transpose2d, the receptive fields overlap differently than for conv2d. For standard conv2d, the "patches" are extracted from the input at stride positions. For conv_transpose2d with stride=1, we want full patches that slide over the input with stride 1, but the content of each patch involves: - For output (h, w), the patch contains input values at (h - dh, w - dw) for dh in [0, K-1], dw in [0, K-1]. This is equivalent to extracting patches from a zero-padded input where the padding is (K_h - 1, K_w - 1) on the "end" side. Actually, it's more like zero-padding on all sides and then extracting. If we zero-pad the input by K-1 on all sides, then for output position (h, w), the corresponding patch in the padded input is at padded position (h, w) with size K_h x K_w, but FLIPPED in spatial dimensions compared to standard conv2d. Actually, if padded_input is input zero-padded by K-1 on all sides: `padded_input[h + dh, w + dw] = input[h + dh - (K_h-1), w + dw - (K_w-1)]` when valid. For output (h, w) in [0, H_out-1] x [0, W_out-1], this corresponds to input positions (h_in, w_in) = (h + dh - K_h + 1, w + dw - K_w + 1). But our desired formula is `out[h, w] = sum_{dh, dw} input[h - dh, w - dw] * w[dh, dw]`. For this to match, we'd need `h - dh = h + dh' - K_h + 1`, which gives `dh' = K_h - 1 - dh`. So `out[h, w] = sum_{dh', dw'} padded_input[h + dh', w + dw'] * w[K_h - 1 - dh', K_w - 1 - dw']`. This is exactly `conv2d(padded_input, flip(w))`! So mathematically, `conv_transpose2d(input, w) = conv2d(pad(input, K-1), flip(w))`. Now, what if I just implement this transformation in my CUDA kernel? I can write a kernel that: 1. Pads the input by (K_h-1, K_w-1) on all sides. 2. Flips the weight. 3. Calls conv2d. But I can't call conv2d from a CUDA kernel easily. For valid indices: `pad_input[h + dh, w + dw] = input[h + dh - K_h + 1, w + dw - K_w + 1]` when 0 <= h + dh - K_h + 1 < H_in. My kernel accumulates 1344 terms in this order: ``` for c_in_b in blocks: for dh: for dw: for c in [0,15]: acc += in * w ``` Each thread accumulates ~1344 terms per output channel in a single `acc` variable. If PyTorch uses a GEMM, the accumulation order would be more like: 1. Multiply patch (1 x 1344) with weight column (1344 x 1) using matrix multiply. 2. The matrix multiply would be tiled, e.g., into 4x4 or 16x16 blocks. 3. Each tile produces a partial sum, which are then summed. Actually, let me check what `F.conv_transpose2d` actually does on a tiny example to make sure. I can't run code, but I can reason through it. If I have: - x = tensor([1.0]).reshape(1, 1, 1, 1) - w = tensor([2.0]).reshape(1, 1, 1, 1) `F.conv_transpose2d(x, w)` should give output of shape (1, 1, 1, 1) = 1.0 * 2.0 = 2.0. If it used `conv2d(pad(x, 0), flip(w))`, pad(x, 0) is just x, flip(w) is w, so conv2d(x, w) = 1.0 * 2.0 = 2.0. Both give the same. For a 1D conv with kernel [a, b]: - x = [1, 0] (input of length 2) - w = [a, b] My formula for conv_transpose: output length = 2+2-1 = 3. out[0] = x[0] * w[0] = a out[1] = x[0] * w[1] + x[1] * w[0] = b out[2] = x[1] * w[1] = 0 If conv2d(pad(x, 1), flip(w)): pad(x, 1) = [0, 1, 0, 0] flip(w) = [b, a] conv2d(pad(x), flip(w)): out[0] = pad[0]*b + pad[1]*a = 0 + a = a out[1] = pad[1]*b + pad[2]*a = b + 0 = b out[2] = pad[2]*b + pad[3]*a = 0 + 0 = 0 They match! So the formulas are equivalent. Now, for PyTorch to compute out[1] = b in the conv2d approach, it computes: `pad[1]*flip_w[0] + pad[2]*flip_w[1] = 1*b + 0*a = b` In my direct formula: `x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0] = 1*b + 0*a = b` Both are the same computation. If PyTorch uses GEMM to compute conv2d, it would compute: `[1, 0, 0] @ [b, a]^T` or some reshaped version. Actually, for conv2d with kernel size 2 and input size 2, the im2col gives: ``` [pad[0], pad[1]] [pad[1], pad[2]] [pad[2], pad[3]] ``` = ``` [0, 1] [1, 0] [0, 0] ``` Multiply by `[b, a]^T` (flipped weight): out[0] = 0*b + 1*a = a out[1] = 1*b + 0*a = b out[2] = 0*b + 0*a = 0 In my direct formula, I compute each output position independently: out[0] = x[0]*w[0] = a out[1] = x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0] = b out[2] = x[1]*w[1] = 0 For out[1], the direct formula computes `x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0]`. The GEMM formula computes `pad[1]*b + pad[2]*a = x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0]`. These are EXACTLY the same! So for this tiny example, both compute the same sequence of operations. So where does the numerical difference come from? For the tiny example, there's no difference because there are only 2 terms. For the large example with 1344 terms, the difference comes from how the 1344 terms are arranged in the GEMM vs how I accumulate them. In im2col + GEMM for conv2d: - The im2col matrix has shape (H_out * W_out, C_in * K_h * K_w) - For conv2d, im2col extracts patches from the padded input. - For our transposed case using conv2d(pad(x), flip(w)): - pad(x) has shape (N, C_in, H_in + K_h - 1, W_in + K_w - 1) - im2col extracts overlapping patches of size (K_h, K_w) with stride 1. - Each output position corresponds to one row of the im2col matrix. - Each row contains `C_in * K_h * K_w` values. - The GEMM computes row @ weight_col where weight_col is shape (C_in * K_h * K_w, C_out). So for each output position and output channel, the computation is: `dot(row, weight_col[:, c_out])` This is exactly a dot product of two vectors of length 1344. The accumulation order depends on how the GEMM tiles the computation. In cuBLAS/cuDNN GEMM, the tiling is typically organized around accessing shared memory efficiently. A common tile size might be, say, 32x32 or 64x64. For our vectors of length 1344, CuBLAS might break it into chunks of 128 or 256. In contrast, my kernel accumulates terms in the order: ``` for c_in in [0, 64): for dh in [0, 3): for dw in [0, 7): dot += ... ``` This is a completely different order. However, the difference of ~0.0005 over 1344 terms is roughly 4e-7 per term, which is about 3-4 ulps in fp32. This is significant but not enormous. For each output channel, PyTorch probably computes it as the sum over input channels of (input_channel ⊛ weight_slice), where ⊛ denotes cross-correlation. For each input channel c_in and output channel c_out: `partial[c_in, c_out] = input[c_in] ⊛ weight[c_in, c_out]` where ⊛ is full cross-correlation (size H+K-1). Then `output[c_out] = sum_{c_in} partial[c_in, c_out]`. In PyTorch, this could be done by: 1. For each (c_in, c_out), compute the 2D cross-correlation of input[c_in] with weight[c_in, c_out] using FFT or direct method. 2. Sum across c_in. If using FFT for each channel pair, the result would be mathematically exact (up to numerical precision of FFT). But FFT introduces O(N log N) complexity and different numerical behavior. If using direct correlation for each pair, each pair involves H*W*K_h*K_w multiply-adds, and there are C_in*C_out pairs. For our case: 64*64*512*512*3*7 ≈ 22.4 billion operations, which is way too many for direct computation without tiling. Wait, but this is the same total operation count as any other algorithm. The filter passed to this function is the same filter used in the forward pass, with descriptor `(out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw)`. If PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d weight is `(in_channels, out_channels, kh, kw)`, PyTorch might PERMUTE the weight to `(out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw)` before passing to cuDNN! If PyTorch transposes the weight (swapping the first two dimensions), then the mathematical operation changes: - Original weight: W[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] - Transposed weight: W^T[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] If cuDNN computes backward data using W^T, the formula would be: `out[c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, dh, dw} in[c_in, h-dh, w-dw] * W^T[c_out, c_in, dh, dw]` = `sum_{c_in, dh, dw} in[...] * W[c_in, c_out, dh, dw]` This is the SAME as the original formula! So permuting the weight dimensions doesn't change the mathematical result because the backward data algorithm is designed to work with the forward weight format. So even if PyTorch permutes the weight before calling cuDNN, the result is the same. I think the permutation is just about data layout, not about the mathematical formula.
