One common pattern in direct convolutions is to process output channels in parallel and accumulate across input channels. Within each input channel, the kernel positions are traversed in a specific order. In cuDNN's implicit GEMM, the M dimension might be output channels, the N dimension might be output spatial positions, and the K dimension is input_channels * K_h * K_w. The K dimension is tiled. If the K tile size is, say, 64, then for each tile, 64 multiply-adds are accumulated before moving to the next tile. The partial sums from tiles are then added. For our case, K = 1344. If tiled by 64, there would be 21 tiles (1344 / 64 = 21). In my current kernel, I effectively have one giant K dimension of 1344. Actually, looking at the test case: - batch_size = 8, in_channels = 64, out_channels = 64, kernel_size = (3, 7), height = 512, width = 512 - stride = 1, padding = 0, output_padding = 0, groups = 1, bias = False PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with these parameters: output shape = (batch_size, out_channels, H_out, W_out) H_out = (512-1)*1 -2*0 + 3 + 0 = 514 W_out = (512-1)*1 -2*0 + 7 + 0 = 518 The reference kernel weights in PyTorch are stored as [in_channels, out_channels/groups, kh, kw] = [64, 64, 3, 7] Now, my kernel's weight is [in_channels, out_channels, kh, kw] = [64, 64, 3, 7] The access: weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K_h + dh) * K_w + dw] This corresponds to weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] - correct for ConvTranspose2d when groups=1. But wait! PyTorch ConvTranspose2d actually implements: output[b, g:o, ...] = sum_i sum_k input[b, g:i+k, ...] * weight[g:i, g:o, k] Actually the relationship between Conv2d and ConvTranspose2d weights is: ConvTranspose2d weight shape is [in_channels, out_channels/groups, *kernel_size] But in the backward pass of Conv2d, the gradient wrt input is computed as a conv transpose. In standard Conv2d: output[n, f, h, w] = sum_c sum_dh sum_dw input[n, c, h+dh, w+dw] * weight[f, c, dh, dw] In ConvTranspose2d (called deconv or fractionally-strided conv): output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_c sum_dh sum_dw input[n, c_in, h', w'] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where h' and w' depend on stride, padding etc. For stride=1, pad=0: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_c sum_{dh=0}^{K_h-1} sum_{dw=0}^{K_w-1} input[n, c, h-(K_h-1-dh), w-(K_w-1-dw)] * weight[c, c_out, dh, dw] Or equivalently: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_c sum_{dh=0}^{K_h-1} sum_{dw=0}^{K_w-1} input[n, c, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c, c_out, K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw] Wait, is the dilation effect? No dilation here. Actually PyTorch ConvTranspose2d implements the transposed convolution, which is equivalent to a full convolution with flipped and transposed filters. But the exact formula implemented by cuDNN might differ from the naive loop. Wait, let me reconsider. PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d can be mathematically computed as: Output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh, dw} Input[n, c_in, h', w'] * Weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where h' and w' are such that applying a standard conv with stride and padding would produce h, w. For stride=1, padding=0, it's simply: h' = h - dh w' = w - dw But if we write this as: for dh in 0..Kh-1: for dw in 0..Kw-1: output += input_shifted_by(dh, dw) * weight[:, :, dh, dw] This is exactly the "direct sum" form. My kernel implements: for dh in 0..Kh-1: for dw in 0..Kw-1: h_num = h_out + pad_h - dh if h_num % stride_h == 0: h_in = h_num / stride_h ... acc += input[n, c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight(...) For stride=1, pad=0: h_num = h_out - dh h_in = h_out - dh w_in = w_out - dw But the valid range for h_in is [0, H_in-1]. So h_out - dh must be in [0, H_in-1]. This means dh must be in [h_out - (H_in-1), h_out]. But dh is in [0, K_h-1]. So for h_out in [0, K_h-1], dh ranges from [0, h_out]. For h_out >= K_h, dh ranges from [h_out-(H_in-1), K_h-1]... wait, that's not right. Actually h_in = h_out - dh. For h_in >= 0 we need dh <= h_out. For h_in < H_in we need dh > h_out - H_in. Since dh is in [0, K_h-1], the valid range is max(0, h_out - H_in + 1) <= dh <= min(K_h - 1, h_out). But in PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d, the typical implementation actually does: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh} sum_{dw} input[n, c_in, h - dh, w - dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] But cuDNN might use: sum over dh and dw of input[..., h+dh, w+dw] * flipped_weight[..., dh, dw] Actually, I realize what's going on. PyTorch Conv2d and ConvTranspose2d have a specific relationship. The weight indices in ConvTranspose2d are related to Conv2d by a reflection. In many implementations, ConvTranspose2d with stride=1 and pad=0 is mathematically equivalent to conv2d with: - stride=1, pad=kernel_size-1, with weight flipped both spatially and channel-wise. Actually, that's only for stride=1 and padding=0. Let me think... Standard conv2d: output[n, f, h, w] = sum_c sum_dh sum_dw input[n, c, h*stride + dh, w*stride + dw] * weight[f, c, dh, dw] For conv transpose with stride=1, pad=0: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_c sum_dh sum_dw input[n, c_in, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] But the PyTorch nn.ConvTranspose2d has a different convention! The weight shape is [in_channels, out_channels/groups, kh, kw], and the actual computation is: it's the transpose of the linear operator defined by conv2d with the same weight but [out_channels, in_channels/groups, kh, kw]. Let me check with a small example. Actually, the documentation says: ConvTranspose2d can be seen as the gradient of Conv2d wrt its input. If we have: y = Conv2d(x, W) Then the VJP: v^T (∂y/∂x) = ConvTranspose2d(v, W) Let's be concrete. Consider 1D case, kernel_size=3, stride=1, pad=0. Conv2d: y[i] = x[i] * w[0] + x[i+1] * w[1] + x[i+2] * w[2] Wait, actually with no padding, for input of length L, output is L-2. y[0] = x[0]*w0 + x[1]*w1 + x[2]*w2 y[1] = x[1]*w0 + x[2]*w1 + x[3]*w2 The Jacobian ∂y/∂x is such that: For v of length L-2, v^T J is a vector of length L where: result[i] = sum_j v[j] * (∂y[j]/∂x[i]) ∂y[j]/∂x[i] is nonzero only when i is in [j, j+2] and i-j = some k where w[k] contributes. Actually y[j] = sum_k x[j+k] * w[k] for k=0,1,2. So ∂y[j]/∂x[i] = w[i-j] if i-j in {0,1,2}, i.e., if j <= i <= j+2. result[i] = sum_{j: j <= i <= j+2} v[j] * w[i-j] = sum_{k=0}^2 v[i-k] * w[k] where v is valid = v[i]*w0 + v[i-1]*w1 + v[i-2]*w2 (with proper boundary handling) So ConvTranspose2d(v, W) = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} v[i-k] * w[k] For 2D, it's: output[h, w] = sum_{dh=0}^{Kh-1} sum_{dw=0}^{Kw-1} input[h-dh, w-dw] * w[dh, dw] This is exactly what my kernel computes for stride=1, pad=0: h_in = h_out - dh w_in = w_out - dw But h_in must be >= 0 and < H_in. So we need h_out >= dh and h_out < H_in + dh. Wait, but h_out ranges from 0 to H_out-1 = H_in + Kh - 2. For h_out near 0: h_in = h_out - dh. For dh > h_out, h_in < 0, so we skip. For h_out near H_out-1: h_in = h_out - dh. For dh < h_out - (H_in-1), h_in > H_in-1, so we skip. My code checks: h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in. That's correct. Let me try a completely different approach: implement the operation as a matrix multiplication using unfold. For transpose convolution, this is: With input of shape [N, C_in, H, W], we can think of each spatial location as a vector of C_in channels. For stride=1, pad=0, ConvTranspose2d with kernel k: Output at position (h,w) gets contributions from input positions (h',w') where h' is in [h-kh+1, h] and w' is in [w-kw+1, w]. Sum contributions. Actually, a ConvTranspose2d can be viewed as: output = conv2d(input_padded, weight_flipped_and_transposed) Where for stride=1, pad=0: - input doesn't need padding for the transpose - weight needs to be: for each output channel, sum over input channels of input[c_in, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] If we pad the input with kh-1 and kw-1 zeros on each side, then do a standard conv2d with stride=1, pad=0: padded_input = pad(input, (kw-1, kw-1, kh-1, kh-1)) output = F.conv2d(padded_input, weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3), ...) For stride=1, a standard conv with the transposed and permuted weight on a padded input: out[h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh,dw} padded_input[c_in, h+dh, w+dw] * weight[c_out, c_in, dh, dw] Where padded_input[c_in, i, j] = input[c_in, i-(kh-1), j-(kw-1)] for valid i,j (after padding). So padded_input[c_in, h+dh, w+dw] = input[c_in, h+dh-(kh-1), w+dw-(kw-1)] = input[c_in, h-(kh-1-dh), w-(kw-1-dw)] If we let dh' = kh-1-dh, dw' = kw-1-dw, then dh = kh-1-dh', dw = kw-1-dw': out[h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh',dw'} input[c_in, h-dh', w-dw'] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh-1-dh', kw-1-dw'] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh',dw'} input[c_in, h-dh', w-dw'] * weight_param[c_in, c_out, kh-1-dh', kw-1-dw'] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh',dw'} input[c_in, h-dh', w-dw'] * weight_flipped[c_in, c_out, dh', dw'] So this is equivalent to my formula but with SPATIALLY FLIPPED weights! Wait, that's an important discovery. To represent ConvTranspose2d as a standard conv2d, you need to: 1. Transpose the channels: [C_in, C_out, kh, kw] -> [C_out, C_in, kh, kw] 2. For Conv2d (cross-correlation): y[i,j] = sum_{dh,dw} x[i+dh, j+dw] * w[dh, dw] Gradient wrt x: dx[u,v] = sum_{i,j} dy[i,j] * (∂y[i,j]/∂x[u,v]) ∂y[i,j]/∂x[u,v] = w[u-i, v-j] if u-i >= 0, v-j >= 0, and within kernel size. dx[u,v] = sum_{i <= u, j <= v} dy[i,j] * w[u-i, v-j] = sum_{dh >= 0, dw >= 0} dy[u-dh, v-dw] * w[dh, dw] This is what I had. BUT WAIT! Method 1: dx = sum dy[u-dh, v-dw] * w[dh, dw] Method 2: out = sum input[u-dh, v-dw] * w[Kh-1-dh, Kw-1-dw] Are these the same operator? If they are supposed to be the transpose of the same conv2d, they must be the same! Let me check: Conv2d: y[i,j] = sum_{dh,dw} x[i+dh, j+dw] * w[dh, dw] The transpose is: T(x)[u,v] = sum_{i,j} x[i,j] * w[u-i, v-j] If we substitute i = u-dh, j = v-dw: T(x)[u,v] = sum_{dh,dw} x[u-dh, v-dw] * w[dh, dw] So Method 1 is the true transpose. But Method 2 computes: T'(x)[u,v] = sum_{dh,dw} x[u-dh, v-dw] * w[Kh-1-dh, Kw-1-dw] For T' to equal T, we would need w[dh, dw] = w[Kh-1-dh, Kw-1-dw] for all dh, dw, i.e., w must be centrally symmetric. This is not true in general! So Method 2 does NOT give the transpose of Conv2d! But wait, the conv2d reformulation I derived was for ConvTranspose2d as implemented by PyTorch. Let me check if PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d actually equals the transpose of Conv2d. In PyTorch, `nn.ConvTranspose2d` is NOT the mathematical transpose of `nn.Conv2d` with the same parameters. It is the transpose of a Conv2d with a DIFFERENT configuration: Specifically, ConvTranspose2d(in, out, k, stride=s, padding=p) is the transpose of Conv2d(out, in, k, stride=s, padding=k-1-p). Wait, let me look this up carefully. Actually: ConvTranspose2d is the adjoint of Conv2d only when the Conv2d has specific parameters. The relationship is that ConvTranspose2d(..., stride, padding) is the transpose of Conv2d(..., stride, kernel_size-1-padding). For stride=1, padding=0, the corresponding Conv2d would have padding=k-1-0=k-1. The transpose of Conv2d with padding=k-1: y[i] = sum_{dh} x[i+dh-(k-1)] * w[dh] = sum_{dh} x[i+dh-k+1] * w[dh] dy/dx[j] = sum_{i: j = i+dh-k+1} dy[i] * w[dh] = sum_{dh} dy[j-dh+k-1] * w[dh] Let dh' = k-1-dh: dy/dx[j] = sum_{dh'} dy[j-(k-1-dh')] * w[k-1-dh'] = sum_{dh'} dy[j-k+1+dh'] * w[k-1-dh'] This is NOT the same as sum dy[j-dh'] * w[dh']. But wait, earlier I proved that the transpose of conv2d gives the formula without spatial flipping. So how can PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d be the transpose of Conv2d if it uses the flipped weight? The answer is: PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d is NOT the transpose of Conv2d with the SAME weight. It is the transpose of Conv2d with a DIFFERENT weight configuration. Actually, in PyTorch, if you create a Conv2d layer and a ConvTranspose2d layer with "corresponding" parameters, ConvTranspose2d is designed to be the approximate inverse. The relationship involves spatial flipping. Actually, I just realized: in PyTorch's implementation, ConvTranspose2d stores the weight in the same format as the corresponding Conv2d layer would, but with in_channels and out_channels swapped. So if Conv2d weight is [out, in, kh, kw], ConvTranspose2d weight is [in, out, kh, kw]. But this "in" and "out" refer to the ConvTranspose2d's input and output channels. So if we think of the underlying conv operator: The Conv2d that ConvTranspose2d is transposing would have weight [out_channels(of convtranspose), in_channels(of convtranspose), kh, kw] = [C_out, C_in, kh, kw]. And ConvTranspose2d stores [C_in, C_out, kh, kw]. For the transpose of Conv2d with weight [C_out, C_in, kh, kw]: The linear operator T is: y = x * W where W is [spatially, channels]. The transpose operator T^T: dx = dy * W^T. In terms of the gradient: If y[f, h, w] = sum_c x[c, h+dh, w+dw] * W[f, c, dh, dw] Then dx[c, u, v] = sum_f sum_{dh,dw} dy[f, u-dh, v-dw] * W[f, c, dh, dw] So the transpose uses W[f, c, dh, dw] directly (no flip) but with dy shifted by dh, dw. But the standard `nn.ConvTranspose2d` computes: output[c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{dh,dw} input[c_in, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] Wait, if we map c_in -> c (input channel) and c_out -> f (output channel): output[f, h, w] = sum_c sum_{dh,dw} input[c, h-dh, w-dw] * weight[c, f, dh, dw] This uses weight[c, f, dh, dw]. If weight is the transpose of the conv weight [f, c, dh, dw], then weight[c, f, dh, dw] = W[f, c, dh, dw]. So this IS the transpose formula! Wait, but I showed earlier that the reformulation as padded conv2d gives SPATIALLY FLIPPED weight. Let me resolve this contradiction. Actually, the key is that F.conv2d uses CROSS-CORRELATION (no flip). So if Conv2d computes: y = x ★ W Then the transpose of this is: dx = dy ★ W (same W, no flip!) But wait, is that true? The transpose of cross-correlation is indeed cross-correlation with the same kernel! Let me verify: y[i] = sum_k x[i+k] * w[k] dx[j] = sum_i dy[i] * ∂y[i]/∂x[j] ∂y[i]/∂x[j] = w[j-i] if j-i is valid. dx[j] = sum_i dy[i] * w[j-i] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] So dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] Compare to y[j] = sum_k x[j+k] * w[k]. These are different! dx[j] shifts dy by -k, while y[j] shifts x by +k. But this can also be written as: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] If we define x'[j] = dy[j], then dx is the cross-correlation of x' with w. But standard cross-correlation is: (x' ★ w)[j] = sum_k x'[j+k] * w[k] That's different from dx[j] = sum_k x'[j-k] * w[k]. Actually, dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] is a CONVOLUTION (with flip), not cross-correlation. But PyTorch calls its operation "conv2d" but implements cross-correlation. So the gradient involves a true convolution with flip. Wait, but earlier I computed the gradient of cross-correlation: y[i] = sum_k x[i+k] * w[k] dx[j] = sum_i dy[i] * w[j-i] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] This is NOT the same as cross-correlation. Cross-correlation would be sum_k dy[j+k] * w[k]. But notice: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k]. If we flip w: w'[k] = w[-k], then: sum_k dy[j+k] * w'[k] = sum_k dy[j+k] * w[-k] = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m] (with m = -k) No wait, that doesn't work with finite bounds. Let me think about this more carefully with finite kernel size. y[i] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[i+k] * w[k] for i in output range. dx[j] = sum_{i: j = i+k for some k in [0,K-1]} dy[i] * w[k] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} dy[j-k] * w[k] (where dy indexes must be valid) Now, define a standard convolution (with flip): (z * w)[j] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} z[j+k] * w[K-1-k] Hmm, this doesn't match either. Actually, define standard cross-correlation in signal processing: (x ⋆ w)[j] = sum_k x[j+k] * w[k] And standard convolution (signal processing): (x * w)[j] = sum_k x[j-k] * w[k] Then dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] = (dy * w)[j], which is CONVOLUTION, not cross-correlation. But PyTorch Conv2d implements cross-correlation: out = x ⋆ w. The gradient with respect to x is: dx = dy * w (convolution, not cross-correlation). Wait, but is standard convolution the same as cross-correlation with a flipped kernel? (x ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_k x[j+k] * w_flipped[k] If w_flipped[k] = w[-k], then: = sum_k x[j+k] * w[-k] = sum_m x[j-m] * w[m] (with m = -k) This matches (x * w)[j] = sum_m x[j-m] * w[m]. But for finite kernels with k in [0, K-1], we need w_flipped[k] = w[K-1-k]. Then: (x ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[j+k] * w[K-1-k] = sum_{m=0}^{K-1} x[j+K-1-m] * w[m] This is NOT the same as (x * w)[j] = sum_m x[j-m] * w[m]. So for finite kernels, convolution is NOT the same as cross-correlation with a flipped kernel (unless we also pad or shift). Coming back to gradients: PyTorch Conv2d (cross-correlation): y = x ⋆ w dx = dy * w (convolution, not cross-correlation) But PyTorch doesn't expose convolution (with flip) directly for Conv2d. The gradient computation in the backward pass uses the appropriate formula. For ConvTranspose2d, PyTorch wants to compute the operation that corresponds to the backward pass of Conv2d wrt input. So ConvTranspose2d(dy) = dy * w (convolution with w). But how does PyTorch implement dy * w using its operators? It can implement it as: 1. Cross-correlation of dy with spatially FLIPPED w: dy ⋆ w_flipped 2. Or using specialized kernels. Actually, if you look at PyTorch internals, the backward of Conv2d wrt input is implemented using the `conv_transpose2d` function in the ATen library. And this function computes the convolution (with flip). So PyTorch ConvTranspose2d computes CONVOLUTION (flip) between input and weight, not cross-correlation. THIS IS THE KEY! My kernel was computing CROSS-CORRELATION (no flip), but PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d effectively computes CONVOLUTION (with spatial flip). Wait, let me verify this with my derivation. I showed: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] And PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d takes dy as input and produces dx as output. So: output[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] where w[k] = weight parameter. Now, if we want to express this as a cross-correlation: output[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] For a standard cross-correlation with flipped weight: (input ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_k input[j+k] * w_flipped[k] If w_flipped[k] = w[-k], this would give sum_k input[j+k] * w[-k] = sum_m input[j-m] * w[m]. But for finite k in [0, K-1]: (input ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} input[j+k] * w_flipped[k] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} input[j+k] * w[K-1-k] = sum_{m=0}^{K-1} input[j+K-1-m] * w[m] This is NOT the same as sum_m input[j-m] * w[m] because of the j+K-1 vs j shift. To make them match, we need to shift the indexing: sum_m input[j-m] * w[m] = sum_m input[(j+K-1)-(m+K-1)] * w[m] If we define j' = j+K-1 and m' = m+K-1: = sum_{m'=K-1}^{2K-2} input[j'-m'] * w[m'-K+1] This doesn't match either. Actually, I think the problem is that I keep mixing up finite support and infinite support. Let me be concrete with a 1D example. y[i] = x[i]*w0 + x[i+1]*w1 (cross-correlation, kernel size=2) for i=0,1: y[0] = x[0]*w0 + x[1]*w1 y[1] = x[1]*w0 + x[2]*w1 Gradient wrt x: dx[0] = dy[0]*w0 dx[1] = dy[0]*w1 + dy[1]*w0 dx[2] = dy[1]*w1 Now, PyTorch ConvTranspose2d with input dy=[a,b], weight=[w0,w1]: output[0] = a*w0 output[1] = a*w1 + b*w0 output[2] = b*w1 Is this equal to conv(dy, weight) where conv is with flip? Convolution with flip: z[i] = sum_k dy[i-k] * w[k] z[0] = dy[0]*w0 = a*w0 z[1] = dy[1]*w0 + dy[0]*w1 = b*w0 + a*w1 z[2] = dy[1]*w1 = b*w1 YES! It matches exactly. Now, cross-correlation (no flip): z[i] = sum_k dy[i+k] * w[k] z[0] = dy[0]*w0 + dy[1]*w1 = a*w0 + b*w1 This is DIFFERENT. So PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d computes CONVOLUTION (with flip), not cross-correlation. And what does my kernel compute? Let's check: my_kernel[h,w] = sum_{dh,dw} input[h-dh, w-dw] * weight[dh, dw] In 1D: out[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] Hey, this IS convolution! My kernel is computing convolution! From PyTorch Docs for ConvTranspose2d: padding controls the amount of implicit zero-paddings on both sides for `dilation * (kernel_size - 1) - padding` number of points. More precisely: The `padding` argument effectively adds `dilation * (kernel_size - 1) - padding` amount of zero padding to both sizes of the input. For our case: padding=0, dilation=1, kernel_size=(3,7). The effective padding added to input is: 1*(3-1) - 0 = 2 (height), and 1*(7-1) - 0 = 6 (width). Wait, this says: for transpose convolution, padding=0 actually means we pad the INPUT with kernel_size - 1 zeros! And the output size is: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding = (512-1) - 0 + 3 = 514 For the full "non-cropped" transpose convolution: If we pad input with 2 zeros on each side, the padded input has height 516. And for standard conv (cross-correlation) on padded input: out[j] = sum_k padded_input[j+k] * w[k] Where padded_input[i] = input[i-2] for i >= 2, and 0 otherwise. So padded_input[j+k] = input[j+k-2] for j+k >= 2. out[j] = sum_k input[j+k-2] * w[k] for valid j+k. Let me re-examine the gradient derivation carefully. Conv2d with stride=1, pad=0, kernel=3 (cross-correlation): y[i] = x[i]*w0 + x[i+1]*w1 + x[i+2]*w2 for i = 0, ..., H-3 (where H is input length). Gradient: dx[j] = sum_i dy[i] * ∂y[i]/∂x[j] ∂y[i]/∂x[j] =: - w0 if j = i - w1 if j = i+1 - w2 if j = i+2 - 0 otherwise dx[j] = dy[j]*w0 + dy[j-1]*w1 + dy[j-2]*w2 (for valid indices) ConvTranspose2d takes dy as input and produces dx: out[j] = in[j]*w0 + in[j-1]*w1 + in[j-2]*w2 For j=0: out[0] = in[0]*w0 (since in[-1]=in[-2]=0) For j=1: out[1] = in[1]*w0 + in[0]*w1 For j=2: out[2] = in[2]*w0 + in[1]*w1 + in[0]*w2 For j=H-1: out[H-1] = in[H-1]*w0 + in[H-2]*w1 + in[H-3]*w2 For j=H: out[H] = in[H-1]*w1 + in[H-2]*w2 (since in[H]=0) For j=H+1: out[H+1] = in[H-1]*w2 This gives output length = H + 2 = H + K - 1. For j=H+1: out[H+1] = in[H-1]*w2. Compare to padded conv2d reformulation: out[0] = in[0]*w2 out[1] = in[0]*w1 + in[1]*w2 out[2] = in[0]*w0 + in[1]*w1 + in[2]*w2 ... out[H+1] = in[H-1]*w0 These are OPPOSITE! The padded conv2d reformulation gives the SPATIALLY FLIPPED result! So the question is: which one does PyTorch compute? If PyTorch computes the GRADIENT of Conv2d (which it claims to), it should compute: out[j] = in[j]*w0 + in[j-1]*w1 + in[j-2]*w2 Which is my first formula. But the padded conv2d reformulation gives: out[j] = in[j]*w2 + in[j-1]*w1 + in[j-2]*w0? No, let me recalculate. Actually, in the padded conv2d reformulation: Input is padded with K-1 zeros on the LEFT. padded_input[i] = 0 for i < K-1 padded_input[i] = input[i-(K-1)] for i >= K-1 Standard conv (cross-correlation) on padded_input: out[j] = sum_k padded_input[j+k] * w[k] For j=0: out[0] = padded_input[0]*w0 + padded_input[1]*w1 + padded_input[2]*w2 = 0*w0 + 0*w1 + input[0]*w2 = input[0]*w2. For j=1: out[1] = padded_input[1]*w0 + padded_input[2]*w1 + padded_input[3]*w2 = 0*w0 + input[0]*w1 + input[1]*w2. For j=2: out[2] = padded_input[2]*w0 + padded_input[3]*w1 + padded_input[4]*w2 = input[0]*w0 + input[1]*w1 + input[2]*w2. This matches the gradient formula IF the weight is [w2, w1, w0] instead of [w0, w1, w2]. Ah! So the padded conv2d reformulation computes the gradient with a FLIPPED weight. If the stored weight is [w0, w1, w2], and we pad the input on the LEFT and do cross-correlation with the SAME weight, we get the gradient with flipped weight. To get the gradient with the original weight, we need to use the RIGHT-padded input and a flipped weight, or LEFT-padded input and the original weight with a different indexing. Actually, I think the correct way to think about it is: - The gradient of cross-correlation is cross-correlation with the same weight... but with the input and output dimensions reversed, and the indexing shifts. Let me think about this more simply. In signal processing: - Cross-correlation: (x ⋆ w)[n] = sum_k x[n+k] * w[k] - Convolution: (x * w)[n] = sum_k x[n-k] * w[k] The gradient of cross-correlation with respect to x is: If y = x ⋆ w, then ∂L/∂x = ∂L/∂y * w (where * is convolution) And convolution x * w is the same as cross-correlation x ⋆ w_flipped, where w_flipped[n] = w[-n]. For finite discrete signals with support [0, K-1]: w_flipped[k] = w[K-1-k]. So ∂L/∂x = ∂L/∂y ⋆ w_flipped. So the gradient of cross-correlation is cross-correlation with the SPATIALLY FLIPPED weight! This means: If y[i] = sum_k x[i+k] * w[k] (cross-correlation) Then dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] (convolution with w) = sum_k dy[j+k] * w_flipped[k] (cross-correlation with w_flipped) = sum_k dy[j+k] * w[K-1-k] But earlier I computed: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k]. And this IS convolution with w. And convolution with w is cross-correlation with flipped(w). So yes! The gradient of cross-correlation is cross-correlation with the flipped weight. Therefore, PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d (which computes the gradient of Conv2d, and Conv2d uses cross-correlation) should compute: output = input ⋆ weight_flipped where weight_flipped[k] = weight[K-1-k]. This means PyTorch SPATIALLY FLIPS the weight in ConvTranspose2d! And THIS explains the numerical difference! My kernel was using the unflipped weight, while PyTorch uses the flipped weight (or equivalently, accesses it in a flipped order). Wait, but I already showed earlier that the gradient formula gives: dx[j] = dy[j]*w0 + dy[j-1]*w1 + dy[j-2]*w2 And this uses the UNFLIPPED weight [w0, w1, w2]. However, this can be written as cross-correlation with a flipped weight when the indexing is adjusted. Let me reconcile: cross-correlation with flipped weight [w2, w1, w0]: (dx ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_k dx[j+k] * w_flipped[k] = sum_k dx[j+k] * w[K-1-k] For K=3: = dx[j]*w2 + dx[j+1]*w1 + dx[j+2]*w0 This is NOT the same as dx[j]*w0 + dx[j-1]*w1 + dx[j-2]*w2. So cross-correlation with flipped weight is DIFFERENT from the gradient I computed. Hmm, let me recalculate the gradient in terms of cross-correlation. Gradient: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] This IS convolution with w, not cross-correlation with flipped w. But if we want to express it as a cross-correlation: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] = sum_m dy[m] * w[j-m] (with m = j-k) And (dy ⋆ w)[j] = sum_k dy[j+k] * w[k]. These are DIFFERENT. (dy ⋆ w)[j] shifts dy by +k, while dx[j] shifts dy by -k. To make them match, define dy'[m] = dy[-m]: Then (dy' ⋆ w)[j] = sum_k dy'[j+k] * w[k] = sum_k dy[-j-k] * w[k]. Still doesn't match. Define w'[k] = w[-k]: (dy ⋆ w')[j] = sum_k dy[j+k] * w'[k] = sum_k dy[j+k] * w[-k]. With m = -k: = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m]. This equals dx[j] = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m] ONLY if the summation range for m is correct. For finite w with support [0, K-1], w'[k] = w[-k] has support [-K+1, 0]. If we define w''[k] = w[K-1-k], then: (dy ⋆ w'')[j] = sum_k dy[j+k] * w[K-1-k] = sum_m dy[j+K-1-m] * w[m] (with m=K-1-k) = sum_m dy[(j+K-1)-m] * w[m] This is dx shifted by K-1: dx[j+K-1]! So (dy ⋆ w'')[j] = dx[j+K-1]. Therefore, dx[j] = (dy ⋆ w'')[j-(K-1)]. This means the gradient dx is equal to the cross-correlation of dy with the flipped weight w''[k]=w[K-1-k], shifted by K-1. Or equivalently, if we pad dy with K-1 zeros on the LEFT and compute cross-correlation with the flipped weight, we get dx. This matches the padded conv2d reformulation! In the padded conv2d reformulation: pad(dy, K-1) conv2d(padded_dy, w_flipped, padding=0) Where w_flipped[k] = w[K-1-k]. For j=0: out[0] = padded_dy[0]*w_flipped[0] + ... = 0 + ... + padded_dy[K-1]*w_flipped[K-1] = dy[0]*w[0]. Wait, padded_dy[K-1] = dy[K-1-(K-1)] = dy[0]. w_flipped[K-1] = w[K-1-(K-1)] = w[0]. out[0] = dy[0]*w[0]. Yes! For j=1: out[1] = padded_dy[1]*w_flipped[0] + padded_dy[2]*w_flipped[1] + ... + padded_dy[K]*w_flipped[K-1] = 0 + ... + padded_dy[K]*w_flipped[K-1] = dy[1]*w[0] + dy[0]*w[1]. This matches my gradient: dx[1] = dy[1]*w0 + dy[0]*w1. So the padded conv2d with LEFT padding and FLIPPED weight DOES produce the correct gradient! But in PyTorch's `nn.ConvTranspose2d`, does it actually pad the input and flip the weight, or does it do something else? Internally, PyTorch calls cuDNN's backward data convolution. cuDNN computes the gradient of a forward convolution. For the forward convolution (cross-correlation), the gradient is computed as directly described by the formula, and cuDNN might implement it using various algorithms (FFT, GEMM, direct). Regardless of the algorithm, the mathematical result is: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] (with proper bounds) And this can be viewed as: 1. Pad dy with K-1 zeros on the LEFT 2. Cross-correlate with the FLIPPED weight 3. Or: pad dy with K-1 zeros on the RIGHT and cross-correlate with the original weight... no, that's not right either. Actually, from my earlier derivation: dx[j] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] = sum_k dy[j-k] * w[k] Compare with: padded_dy[x] = dy[x-(K-1)] for x >= K-1. (padded_dy ⋆ w)[j] = sum_k padded_dy[j+k] * w[k] = sum_k dy[j+k-(K-1)] * w[k] (for valid j+k >= K-1) = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m+(K-1)] (with m = K-1-k, k = K-1-m) This doesn't match. But (padded_dy ⋆ w_flipped)[j] = sum_k padded_dy[j+k] * w_flipped[k] = sum_k padded_dy[j+k] * w[K-1-k] = sum_k dy[j+k-(K-1)] * w[K-1-k] (for j+k >= K-1) let m = K-1-k, then k = K-1-m: = sum_m dy[j+(K-1-m)-(K-1)] * w[m] = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m] YES! This exactly matches dx[j] = sum_m dy[j-m] * w[m]. So the gradient can be computed as: LEFT-pad dy with K-1 zeros, then cross-correlate with the SPATIALLY FLIPPED weight. This means: PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d effectively FLIPS the weight spatially compared to a "naive" interpretation! And THIS is why my kernel results differ from PyTorch's reference. My kernel was computing: out[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] But it should have been computing: out[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] Wait, that's the SAME formula! The difference is whether w[k] refers to the stored weight or the flipped weight. In my kernel, I accessed `weight[dh, dw]` where dh and dw are the loop indices. But if PyTorch effectively flips the weight, then the computation should use `weight[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw]`. My kernel computed: acc += input[h-dh, w-dw] * weight[dh, dw] PyTorch computes: acc += input[h-dh, w-dw] * weight[K_h-1-dh, K_w-1-dw] This would explain the systematic difference! Because only one input element contributes, and it's always multiplied by different weights in sequence. I think the correct behavior is that ConvTranspose2d with w=[1,0,0] should "delay" the signal, while Conv2d with w=[1,0,0] should "advance" it. The transpose should reverse the effect of the shift. Conv2d with stride=1, pad=0, w=[1,0,0]: y[i] = x[i] * 1 + x[i+1] * 0 + x[i+2] * 0 = x[i] So y = x[0:-2] (the first H-2 elements). The transpose of this operator should reconstruct the original x from y. Since y only contains the first H-2 elements, the transpose should map y back to a vector where the first H-2 elements are preserved, and the last 2 elements are zero (because they were discarded in the forward pass). So the transpose should give: [y[0], y[1], ..., y[H-3], 0, 0] = [x[0], x[1], ..., x[H-3], 0, 0] For input x=[a,b], H=2: y = [a] (since H-2 = 0, y has length 0... wait, for H=2 and K=3, H-K+1 = 0, so y is empty). To get [p, q, 0, 0] from [p, q] with w=[1,0,0], we need: out[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] Can this be written as a standard conv? Let's see: out[j] = sum_k input[j-k] * w[k] = sum_m input[m] * w[j-m] (with m = j-k) For a standard conv (cross-correlation): (padded_input ⋆ w')[j] = sum_k padded_input[j+k] * w'[k] If padded_input is RIGHT-padded with K-1 zeros: padded_input[m] = input[m] for m < H padded_input[m] = 0 for m >= H And if we use w'[k] = w[k] (no flip): (padded_input ⋆ w)[j] = sum_k padded_input[j+k] * w[k] = sum_k input[j+k] * w[k] for j+k < H = sum_m input[m] * w[m-j] for m >= j = sum_m input[m] * w[m-j] This is NOT the same as sum_m input[m] * w[j-m]. For a GEMM C = A @ B, each element C[i,j] = sum_k A[i,k] * B[k,j]. The summation is over k from 0 to K-1. For ConvTranspose2d reformulated as GEMM: - A = input unrolled (or im2col transposed): [N*H_out*W_out, C_in*K_h*K_w] - B = weight reshaped: [C_in*K_h*K_w, C_out] - C = output: [N*H_out*W_out, C_out] In this GEMM, the accumulation order is over the single dimension k from 0 to C_in*K_h*K_w-1. In my kernel's accumulation, the order is nested: c, dh, dw or dh, dw, c. If I change to a single flat loop over c*K_h*K_w in a specific order, it might better match the GEMM order. For example: ```cuda for (int idx = 0; idx < C_in * K_h * K_w; ++idx) { int c = idx / (K_h * K_w); int dh = (idx / K_w) % K_h; int dw = idx % K_w; // ... } ``` This accumulates in order of a flattened index. If the GEMM uses this exact order (row-major unrolling), then the results might match! Let me try this. Actually, this is just a different nesting order. Let me trace through the exact formula one more time. In a ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0, the output at (h, w) is: sum_{c_in} sum_{dh=0}^{Kh-1} sum_{dw=0}^{Kw-1} input[c_in, h', w'] * weight[c_in, c_out, dh, dw] where h' and w' depend on the transposed convolution definition. In PyTorch's docs for ConvTranspose2d, the formula for h' is: h' = (h + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - output_padding) / stride + 1? No, that's the inverse. For the forward of conv transpose: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 The actual indexing in the transposed convolution is related to the "fractionally-strided" convolution. If we think of the transposed convolution as: 1. Take the input 2. Insert stride-1 zeros between elements (for stride > 1) 3. Pad with `kernel_size - 1 - padding` on each side 4. Run a standard convolution with the SAME weight (but possibly flipped) Wait, for stride=1: - No zeros inserted. - Pad with `kernel_size - 1 - padding` = `3 - 1 - 0 = 2` on each side for height. - Run standard conv with weight... but with FLIP! Actually, the standard formulation is: ConvTranspose2d(input, weight, stride=s, padding=p, output_padding=op) is equivalent to: 1. Pad input with `dilation * (kernel_size - 1) - padding` zeros on each side. For dilation=1: `kernel_size - 1 - padding`. 2. Convolve the padded input with weight flipped over both spatial dimensions. 3. Crop `output_padding` elements from one side. For our test: padding=0, kernel_size=3. Pad with 2 zeros on each side. Then convolve with flipped weight. For a GEMM C = A @ B, where dimensions are M = N*H*W, K = C_in*K_h*K_w, N = C_out. Each C[i,j] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} A[i,k] * B[k,j]. In cuBLAS/cuDNN's GEMM implementation, the K dimension is typically divided into tiles (e.g., 32 or 64 elements). Each tile is loaded into shared memory or registers, and the accumulation happens tile by tile. Within each tile, the order might be sequential or depend on the thread layout. So the reference's accumulation order is: tile_0_sum + tile_1_sum + tile_2_sum + ... And within each tile, the order depends on the thread mapping. Use `im2col` on the input (but for transpose, it's actually a different unfolding). Actually, for transposed convolution with stride=1, the operation can be seen as: - Input: [N, C_in, H, W] - We want output: [N, C_out, H_out, W_out] The relationship is: each output pixel (h, w) gets contributions from input pixels (h', w') such that applying a standard conv with stride=1, pad=0 to the output would recover the input at (h', w') with the appropriate weight. For standard conv: input'[h', w'] = sum_{dh, dw} output[h'+dh, w'+dw] * weight[dh, dw] So for transpose: we want to solve for output given input'. This can be written as a linear system where each input' element contributes to multiple output elements. The system matrix is the convolution matrix C where C[(h',w'), (h,w)] = weight[h-h', w-w'] if valid. The transpose of this system matrix is C^T. And applying C^T to input' gives the output. C^T can be implemented as a GEMM if we reshape the problem appropriately. Specifically, define A of shape [N*H_in*W_in, C_in] where A contains input values. Define B of shape [C_in, C_out*K_h*K_w]? Not quite. Actually, the standard way to implement ConvTranspose2d as GEMM: 1. The weight W of shape [C_in, C_out, Kh, Kw] is reshaped to [C_in, C_out*Kh*Kw]. 2. The input X of shape [N, C_in, H, W] is reshaped to [N*H*W, C_in]. 3. Compute Y = X @ W of shape [N*H*W, C_out*Kh*Kw]. 4. Apply col2im to Y to get the output of shape [N, C_out, H_out, W_out]. Wait, that's for standard Conv2d using im2col. For ConvTranspose2d, it's actually the opposite. Actually, ConvTranspose2d with stride=1 can be written as: 1. The weight W [C_in, C_out, Kh, Kw] -> reshape to [C_in*Kh*Kw, C_out]. 2. For each pixel (h, w) in the input, take the Kh x Kw neighborhood in the OUTPUT that it contributes to. 3. This is essentially `im2col` but in the opposite direction. Hmm, the exact im2col for transpose is a bit more involved. Usually, libraries implement it by reshaping the weight to [C_out, C_in*Kh*Kw] and then using a specialized `col2im` after the GEMM. Let me look at the standard formulation: For ConvTranspose2d: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh, kw} input[n, c_in, h-kh, w-kw] * weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] If we define for each output position (h, w), the set of contributing input positions, we can write this as a matrix-vector product. But the matrix would be very large. Alternatively, we can write it as: For each input position (h_in, w_in) and channel c_in, it contributes to output positions (h_in+kh, w_in+kw) for kh in [0, Kh-1], kw in [0, Kw-1], with weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw]. So we can think of this as: 1. For each spatial position, compute a matrix product between the input channels at that position and the weight. 2. Scatter the results to the appropriate output positions. Specifically: For each (h_in, w_in): input_vec = input[:, h_in, w_in] of shape [N, C_in] Compute output_patch = input_vec @ weight_reshape # where weight_reshape is [C_in, C_out*Kh*Kw] # output_patch shape: [N, C_out*Kh*Kw] Then scatter output_patch into output[:, :, h_in:h_in+Kh, w_in:w_in+Kw] If we iterate over all (h_in, w_in), each output position (h, w) receives contributions from exactly the input positions (h-kh, w-kw) that are valid. This is a valid implementation! And the GEMM part uses `torch.matmul`. For the scatter part, we need to use either atomicAdd or a reduction. Since each output position receives multiple contributions, we need to reduce them. With atomicAdd in FP32, the order is non-deterministic based on thread execution order. This would give a very different result from sequential summation. To make it deterministic, we could use a temporary accumulation buffer and then reduce. But this is complex. Alternatively, we can transpose the iteration: for each output position, gather the contributing input positions and compute the sum directly. This is exactly what my custom kernel does. So the GEMM approach doesn't help with matching unless we use a deterministic reduction. For a sum of N terms each with relative error ~eps, the absolute error in the sum is ~N * eps * average_term_magnitude (for sequential summation of same-sign terms).
